Excused Amounts of Heavy and Light Impurities
Explanation of the correct amount of impurity excused in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the excused amounts for heavy and light impurities and distinguish between the different Hanafi views on the measurement of light impurity.

Core Idea
A dirham of heavy impurity and less than a quarter of the garment of light impurity are excused in prayer.

The Hanafi school provides clear guidelines on the amount of impurity that is pardoned during prayer, balancing ritual purity and practical ease. This lesson unpacks the precise measurements: a dirham weight for heavy impurities and a quarter of the garment or body for light impurities. An internal Hanafi dispute centers on whether the quarter is measured from the whole garment or just the affected area, and the preferred view is established through analogy (qiyas) to the quarter of the head wiped in wudu.

Guided Path
1
State the excused amount for heavy impurity
Identify the authoritative Hanafi ruling: a dirham weight (about 3.125g) of heavy impurity is excused. Note that this is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
Heavy impurities include human urine and feces.
2
State the excused amount for light impurity
Less than a quarter of the whole garment or body is excused. This is the corrected view in the text.
Light impurities include urine of halal animals.
3
Identify the internal dispute
Some Hanafi scholars (e.g., al-Aqta') measure the quarter from only the affected area rather than the whole garment. Note the arguments for each.
This dispute is about the 'quarter of what' – the whole or the part.
4
Analyze the qiyas for the quarter
The analogy to wiping a quarter of the head in wudu shows that a quarter stands in place of the whole, supporting the whole-garment measurement.
The same principle applies to shaving and purification.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Al-Tahtawi and earlier masters preferred the quarter of the whole garment/body, as it includes both garment and body, and is more practical.
This resolves the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) for valid prayer.

The Book of Purification. The fingers as understood by al-Hindwani, and it is the correct [view]; thus, excused from heavy impurity is [up to a dirham], so it is not excused if it exceeds a dirham while able to remove it. And excused is an amount less than a quarter of the complete garment or the whole body, according to the correct [view] for light impurity, because the quarter stands in place of the whole, like wiping a quarter of the head, shaving it, and purifying a quarter of the cover. And from Imam [Abu Hanifa]: a quarter of the least garment in which prayer is valid, like a waist-wrap. Imam al-Baghdadi said: the well-known [view] of al-Aqta' is this. The most authentic transmitted about it, but it is limited to the garment. And it was said: a quarter of the affected area, like the hem and the sleeve. He said in al-Tuhfah: it is the most authentic, and in al-Haqa'iq, and upon it is the fatwa. And other [views] were mentioned. He said: (and it is the correct [view]) al-Zayla'i and others declared it correct, and it was confirmed in al-Fath, and the general scholars chose it because applying both narrations when possible is preferable, especially with the appropriateness of this distribution, as in al-Bahr. He said: (so that is excused) i.e., because the correct view is what was mentioned, the weighty dirham from heavy impurity is excused. He said: (and excused is less than a quarter of the garment) I have not seen clarification regarding the dislikedness when it is less than a quarter, whether it is prohibited or discouraged. He said: (a quarter of the complete garment is the chosen [view]) as in al-Durr from al-Halabi, and it was said in al-Mabsut: it is the most authentic. He said: (the quarter standing in place of the whole) is a cause for something elided, i.e., the quarter is not excused because it stands in place of the whole in matters like wiping, etc., so it is an example of something elided. He said: (and shaving it) means it is forbidden, and one must offer a sacrifice, and he may be released from it by shaving. He said: (and it was said: a quarter of the affected area) and the first [view] is better for conveying the ruling of the body and garment, and because the affected area is not much, let alone excessive. Due to the weakness of this opinion, it was not relied upon in al-Fath, as in al-Nahr, although it was said in al-Haqa'iq: and upon it is the fatwa, as in al-Durr. Al-Kamal said: what appears is that the first [view] is better, except that if that garment is comprehensive, its quarter is considered, and if it is the least in which prayer is valid, its quarter is considered because it is much relative to the affected garment.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the excused amounts of najasah. The author clarifies the correct opinion regarding heavy (maghladhah) and light (khafifah) impurities. For heavy impurities, the excused amount is the weight of a dirham (about 3.125 grams), as affirmed by al-Hindwani and others. For light impurities, the excused amount is less than a quarter of the garment or body, based on the principle that a quarter stands in place of the whole, analogous to wiping a quarter of the head in wudu. There is disagreement on whether the quarter is measured from the full garment or the affected part. The preferred view (as per al-Tahtawi) is that for a comprehensive garment, its quarter is considered; for a minimal garment, its quarter is also considered. This allows for practicality in prayer.

Key Terms
النجاسة المغلظة (al-najasah al-mughallazhah): Heavy impurity, such as urine and feces of humans and animals whose meat is not eaten.
النجاسة الخفيفة (al-najasah al-khafifah): Light impurity, such as urine of animals whose meat is eaten, or small amounts of heavy impurity.
الدرهم (al-dirham): A weight measure traditionally equivalent to about 3.125 grams; used to define the excused amount of heavy impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi view, what is the excused amount for light impurity?
A) Less than a dirham
B) Less than a quarter of the complete garment
C) Less than a quarter of the affected area
D) Any amount if less than the size of a coin

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school excuses up to a dirham of heavy impurity and less than a quarter of the garment (or body) of light impurity, with the preferred view measuring the quarter against the whole garment, not just the affected area.

The Excused Splash of Urine and Unavoidable Impurities
Explanation of the ruling on splash of urine and its extension to other unavoidable impurities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the splash of urine, identify the size limit for the excused amount, and explain the basis of hardship (haraj) that justifies the leniency.

Core Idea
Splash of urine is excused due to unavoidable hardship, even if it covers the entire garment.

The splash of urine (rashash) is a common but minor impurity that can occur during urination. Hanafi fiqh, recognizing the difficulty of complete avoidance, excuses such splashes under certain conditions. This lesson examines the legal ruling, its proof, its size limitation, and its extension to similar unavoidable impurities such as the washing water of the deceased.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act in question is the presence of a splash of urine on the body, garment, or place. The ruling is that it is excused (mu‘afan) – it does not nullify prayer or require washing.
The term 'excused' means the impurity is overlooked for the validity of the prayer.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof cited is the statement of Ibn Abbas (mawquf) recorded in al-Siraj: 'We hope from Allah something more expansive than this.' This indicates that the general mercy of Allah covers the difficulty of avoiding such splashes.
The proof is a mursal hadith, but it is accepted as a basis for leniency due to hardship.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal implication)
The phrase 'more expansive' implies that the ruling is not strict – hardship (haraj) is lifted. The impossibility of complete avoidance, especially in windy conditions, justifies the leniency.
This is a direct application of the principle 'al-mashaqqah tajlib al-taysīr' (hardship brings ease).
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf (narrated by al-Mu‘alla) holds that if the trace of the splash is visible, washing is required. The majority, including the author, side with the unconditional excuse.
The mufta bihi is the majority view, as adopted by al-Tahtawi and others.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its scope
The relied-upon position: the splash is excused unconditionally as long as the quantity is small – defined as the size of the head of a needle. If it reaches the size of the head of a skewer, it is not excused. This leniency also applies to similar unavoidable impurities like the washing water of the deceased and to impure oil that spreads after contact.
The size limit is a visual measure; anything larger must be washed before prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وعفي رشاش بول) انتضح على بدن، أو ثوب أو مكان كما أفاده مسكين وخرج بذلك الماء القليل فإنه يفسده حتى لو سقط ذلك الثوب مثلاً فيه نجسه، وقيل: لا لأنه لما سقط اعتبار هذه النجاسة عم الثوب، والماء والأول أصح لأن سقوط اعتبارها كان للحرج، ولا حرج في الماء كما في الحلبي عن الكفاية وروى المعلى في نوادره عن يوسف أنه إن كان یری أثره ه لا بد من غسله. قوله : (كرؤوس الإبر) بكسر ففتح جمع إبرة كسدرة وسدر، وفي التقييد بها إشارة إلى أنه لو كان مثل رؤوس المسال منع، بلا خلاف . قوله: (للضرورة) لأنه لا يمكن الإحتراز عنه لا سيما في مهب الريح فسقط اعتباره وقد سئل ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما عن هذا فقال : إنا لنرجو من الله تعالى أوسع من هذا كما في السراج قوله : (لا ينجسه) سواء كان الماء جارياً أو راكداً لأن الغالب أن الرشاش المتصاعد عما لا يمكن الاحتراز عنه من غسالة الميت ما دام في علاجه لعموم البلوى وبعد اجتماعها ما أصابته وإذا انبسط الدهن النجس فزاد عن القدر المعفو عنه لا يمنع في اختيار المرغيناني وجماعة بالنظر لوقت الإصابة ومختار غيرهم المنع فإن صلى قبل اتساعه صحت أخذ الأكثرون كما في السراج الوهاج
He said: And excused is the splash of urine that splashes on the body, garment, or place, as stated by Miskin, and thus excludes a small quantity of water because it nullifies it; so if that garment, for example, falls into it, it becomes impure. And it was said: no, because when the consideration of this impurity dropped, [it] generalizes the garment and water; and the first is more correct because the dropping of its consideration was due to hardship, and there is no hardship in water, as in al-Halabi from al-Kifayah. And al-Mu'alla narrated in his Nawadir from Yusuf: if he sees its trace, he must wash it. He said: like the heads of needles (plural of ibrah), and by specifying this, it indicates that if it were like the heads of skewers, it would be prohibited without disagreement. He said: due to necessity, because it cannot be avoided, especially in the path of the wind, so its consideration drops. And Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them both) was asked about this and said: 'We hope from Allah Ta'ala something more expansive than this' as in al-Siraj. He said: it does not make it impure, whether the water is flowing or stagnant, because usually the splash that rises is from that which cannot be avoided from the washing water of the deceased as long as he is being treated, due to general affliction, and after its accumulation, what it hits [becomes impure?]. And if impure oil spreads and increases beyond the excused amount, it does not prevent [prayer] according to the choice of al-Marghinani and a group, considering the time of contact, while the choice of others is prevention; if he prayed before its spread, his prayer is valid according to the majority, as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on the splash (rashash) of urine. Because it is impossible to avoid, the splash is excused even if it covers the entire body or garment. The analogy of 'heads of needles' indicates that the amount must be small; if it is larger (like heads of skewers), it is not excused without dispute. The proof cited is the statement of Ibn Abbas that Allah's mercy is vast, implying leniency. The passage also extends this leniency to the washing water of the deceased (ghassalah) and even to impure oil spreading beyond the excused amount—according to al-Marghinani, it does not invalidate salah if the increase occurred after the contact. The majority hold that if the oil increases after prayer, the prayer is still valid. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle of hardship (haraj) justifying leniency.

Key Terms
رشاش بول (rashash bawl): Splash of urine that sprays onto a person or garment involuntarily.
رؤوس الإبر (ru'us al-ibar): The heads of needles, used as a measure for the maximum size of excusable splash; smaller than the head of a skewer.
غسالة الميت (ghassalah al-mayyit): The water used to wash a deceased person, considered impure but excused due to general affliction.
عموم البلوى (umum al-balwa): General affliction – a situation that is widespread and cannot be avoided, which justifies legal leniency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the maximum size of excusable splash of urine according to the passage?
A) Less than a quarter of the garment
B) The size of a dirham
C) The size of the head of a needle
D) Any size if it covers the entire body

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school excuses unavoidable splashes of urine and similar impurities due to hardship, provided the amount is no larger than the head of a needle and the impurity is not in a small quantity of water.

Ruling on Street Mud and Wet Surfaces with Impurity
Clarification of the Hanafi ruling on street mud and wet surfaces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the purity of street mud and identify when it is considered excused.

Core Idea
Street mud is excused unless actual impurity is visible — necessity lightens the ruling.

When you walk in the market, your feet will inevitably encounter mud that may be contaminated. The Hanafi school provides a practical ruling based on general affliction (umum al-balwa). In this lesson, we examine a passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi that clarifies the correct view: street mud and places frequented by dogs are pure unless you see the impurity with your own eyes. Even if the ground is known to be impure, if no trace appears on your foot or garment, your salah remains valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
According to Abu Nasr al-Dabusi as cited in the passage, what is the default ruling of street mud and places of dogs?
The passage states they are 'tahir' (pure) unless the actual impurity is seen.
2
Note the exception
Under what condition does the mud become impure? Look for the phrase 'unless he sees the actual impurity'.
The condition is visual certainty of najasah.
3
Understand the lexical distinction
What does 'ardaghah' mean and why is it broader than 'wahl'? The passage explains the linguistic nuance.
Ardaghah includes water and mud, while wahl is thin mud only.
4
Apply to the sleeping scenario
How does the rule apply to a person lying on an impure rug? If the person's foot or body becomes wet and the impurity's trace appears, then it becomes impure; otherwise, mere dampness does not transfer impurity.
This reinforces the principle that visibility of the trace matters.
5
Recognize the critical refinement
How does al-Tahtawi refine the wording from al-Durr al-Mukhtar? He notes that the excuse is the correct view, contrary to the phrasing in al-Durr that narrated it as 'qil' (it was said).
Al-Tahtawi asserts that the excuse is the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو مشى في السوق الخ) قال في المنح عن أبي نصر الدبوسي : طين الشوارع، ومواطن الكلاب ،طاهر، وكذا الطين المسرقن إلا إذا رأى عين النجاسة. قال رحمه الله تعالى: وهو الصحيح اهـ أي من حيث الدراية، وقريب من حيث الرواية عن أصحابنا رضي الله عنهم، وفي الدر المختار وغيره وعفى طين شارع ومواطن كلاب وبخار ،نجس وغبار سرقين وانتضاح غسالة لا تظهر مواقع قطرها في الماء اهـ وظاهر ذلك ان العفو مصحح خلافاً لما تفيده عبارته فإنه حكاه بقيل. قوله : ( وردغة الطين) الردغة محركة وتسكن الماء والطين والوحل الشديد والجمع كصحب وخدم قاموس وفيه الوحل ويحرك الطين الرقيق اهـ فالمراد بالردغة في كلامه هو بالمعنى الأول، وهو الماء والطين فإنه أعم من الوحل لأنه الطين الرقيق، فلا يقال له وحل إلا إذا امتزج بخلاف الردغة وليحرر قوله : (من عرق نائم قيد اتفاقي فالمستيقط كذلك القدم ولو وضع قدمه الجاف الطاهر، أو نام على نحو بساط نجس رطب

He said: (and if he walks in the market etc.) He said in al-Manh from Abu Nasr al-Dabusi: The mud of streets and places of dogs are pure, and likewise the mud of dunghills unless he sees the actual impurity. He said (may Allah have mercy on him): and it is the correct [view], i.e., from the perspective of understanding, and similar is the narration from our companions (may Allah be pleased with them). In al-Durr al-Mukhtar and others: Excused is the mud of streets, places of dogs, steam of impurity, and dust of dung, and the splashing of washing water that does not show the locations of its drops in the water. And the apparent meaning is that the excuse validates, contrary to what his statement conveys, for he narrated it with 'qil'. He said: (and the mud [ardaghah]) al-ardaghah (with damma and sukun) is water and mud and severe mire, plural like sahb and khidam. In the dictionary: al-wahl (mire) with a fatha means thin mud. So the meaning of ardaghah in his speech is the first meaning, water and mud, for it is more general than wahl, because wahl is thin mud, so it is not called wahl unless it mixes, unlike ardaghah. Let it be verified. He said: (from the sweat of a sleeping person is an incidental condition; the awake person is similar. And if a person places his dry, pure foot, or sleeps on a wet impure rug, etc., it is understood that if what touches that becomes wet, it becomes impure; otherwise not. And mere dampness is not considered in the chosen view, as in al-Siraj from al-Fatawa. He said: (upon both) i.e., upon who sleeps on the impure bed or soil. He said: (or if)
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage deals with the practical scenario of walking in the market where the ground may be wet with impure water. The correct Hanafi view, attributed to Abu Nasr al-Dabusi, is that street mud and places frequented by dogs are presumed pure (tahir) unless one sees the actual impurity. Similarly, dust from dung heaps is excused unless the impurity itself is visible. The term 'ardaghah' refers to a mixture of water and mud, which is more general than 'wahl' (thin mud). The passage also discusses the case of a person lying on a wet impure rug: if the person's body becomes wet and the impurity's trace (taste, color, or smell) appears, then the body becomes impure; otherwise, it does not. Mere dampness without trace is not considered.

Key Terms
طين الشوارع (teen al-shawari'): The mud found on public streets, considered pure unless the actual impurity is seen.
مواطن الكلاب (mawatin al-kilab): Places where dogs frequent, which are presumed pure unless impurity is visible.
ردغة الطين (ardaghah al-teen): A mixture of water and mud, thicker than simple mud (wahl), including severe mire.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When one sees the actual impurity (e.g., feces) with certainty
C) Only when the mud is wet
D) Never, it is always considered pure

Key Takeaway
Street mud is excused by default; only visible impurity nullifies the excuse.

The Ruling on Ghassalat al-Mayyit (Wash Water of a Corpse)
Ruling on impurity of corpse wash water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the wash water of a corpse during and after washing, and explain the basis of the Hanafi concession and the ikhtilaf regarding the nature of the corpse's impurity.

Core Idea
Corpse wash water is exempt during washing due to necessity, but impure after collection unless the corpse's impurity is ritual only.

This lesson examines a practical issue in purification: the legal status of the water used to wash a deceased body. The Hanafi school offers a nuanced ruling that balances ritual purity with the reality of general necessity (ʿumūm al-balwā). The passage from the Hashiya of al-Tahtawi clarifies both the default ruling and an important intra-madhhab difference regarding whether the corpse's impurity is substantive or ritual.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its temporal phases
The ruling applies to the wash water in two distinct phases: while it is being used on the corpse (during treatment) and after it has been collected.
The phrase 'ما دام في علاجه' marks the period of concession.
2
State the ruling for each phase
During washing: the water is excused (maʿfū) — it does not render things impure. After collection: it becomes impure (najis) and contaminates whatever it touches.
The concession is due to ʿumūm al-balwā (general widespread occurrence).
3
Analyze the basis of the ruling after collection
The impurity of the collected water rests on the view that the corpse's impurity is substantive (khabath). If instead it is only ritual (hadath) and the body is free from substantive impurity, the wash water remains pure.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the nature of the corpse's impurity.
4
Apply the principle of ʿumūm al-balwā
ʿUmūm al-balwā is a Hanafi principle that permits leniency for unavoidable impurities. Here it allows the wash water to be overlooked during the washing process.
This principle is used in many taharah rulings, e.g., for mud on streets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة عما لا يمكن الاحتراز عنه من غسالة الميت ما دام في علاجه لعموم البلوى وبعد اجتماعها ما أصابته. قوله : (من غسالة الميت) أي مطلقاً ولو كان على بدنه نجاسة كما في الفتح. قوله : ( تنجس ما أصابته هذا بناء على القول بأن نجاسته نجاسة خبث، وأما على القول بأنها نجاسة حدث، وتيقن طهارة بدنه من خبث ، فغسالته طاهرة
The Book of Purity: [Regarding] that which cannot be avoided from the wash water of a corpse as long as it is in its treatment, due to general necessity, and after its collection, whatever it touches becomes impure. He said concerning 'from the wash water of the corpse': it applies absolutely, even if there is impurity on his body, as in al-Fath. He said concerning 'impure what it touches': this is based on the view that its impurity is substantive (khabath); but on the view that it is ritual impurity (hadath) and the body is certain to be free of substantive impurity, then its wash water is pure.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with supporting reasoning from the Hashiya of al-Tahtawi on Maraqi al-Falah. The matn states that the wash water of a corpse is excused during the washing process due to general necessity (ʿumūm al-balwā), but once collected, it renders anything it touches impure. The hashia clarifies that this applies regardless of whether the corpse had other impurities on its body. It also notes that the impure status of the wash water depends on the nature of the corpse's impurity: if it is substantive (khabath), the water is impure; if it is ritual (hadath) and the body is known to be clean of substantive impurities, then the water is pure. The ruling aligns with the Hanafi principle that rulings are based on the predominant situation unless evidence indicates otherwise.

Key Terms
غسالة الميت (ghasālat al-mayyit): Wash water used to cleanse a deceased person's body.
عموم البلوى (ʿumūm al-balwā): General widespread occurrence that makes avoidance impossible.
نجاسة خبث (najāsat khabath): Substantive impurity caused by physical substances like blood, urine, or feces.
نجاسة حدث (najāsat hadath): Ritual impurity resulting from states like janabah, menstruation, or lack of wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the concession (ʿafw) on the wash water during the treatment of a corpse?
A) The corpse is considered pure.
B) General necessity (ʿumūm al-balwā).
C) The water is mixed with soap.
D) The washing is a communal obligation.

Key Takeaway
The wash water of a corpse is excused during washing due to general necessity, but after collection it is impure unless the corpse's impurity is purely ritual.

Spread of Najis Oil and Validity of Prayer
Ruling on excusing spread of impure oil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the spread of najis oil beyond the excused amount and identify when the spread does not invalidate prayer.

Core Idea
Timing matters: prayer performed before impurity spread remains valid.

This passage addresses a common concern: what happens when impure oil on one's body or garment spreads beyond the excusable limit during prayer? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling that considers the timing of the spread relative to the original contact. Understanding this helps avoid unnecessary invalidation of prayers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The act in question is the spread of najis oil on the body or garment during prayer. The ruling concerns whether this spread invalidates the prayer.
Najis oil means oil that has become impure due to contact with an impurity.
2
State the two positions
Al-Marghinani and a group hold that the spread does not prevent validity if considered from the time of initial contact. Others hold that it does prevent validity.
The ikhtilaf is intra-Hanafi.
3
Identify the majority view
The majority rule that if the prayer was performed before the spread occurred, it is valid. This is the relied-upon view.
This is the mufta bihi according to the text.
4
Extract the underlying principle
The principle is that the spread after the initial contact is excused, as long as the oil was within the excused amount at the time of prayer.
This reflects the Hanafi leniency in taharah matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا انبسط الدهن النجس فزاد عن القدر المعفو عنه لا يمنع في اختيار المرغيناني وجماعة بالنظر لوقت الإصابة ومختار غيرهم المنع فإن صلى قبل اتساعه صحت أخذ الأكثرون كما في السراج الوهاج
And if the najis oil spreads and increases beyond the excused amount, it does not prevent (the validity of prayer) in the choice of al-Marghinani and a group, considering the time of contact; the choice of others is that it does prevent. If one prayed before its expansion, it is valid, according to the majority, as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-madhhab difference (ikhtilaf) on whether the spread of najis oil beyond the excused amount invalidates prayer. Al-Marghinani and some hold that if the spread occurred after the initial contact (i.e., after the time of the original occurrence), it does not affect the prayer. Others, including some Hanafi scholars, say it does prevent validity. The majority, however, rule that if the prayer was performed before the oil spread, it is valid. This emphasizes the Hanafi principle of considering the time of contact and the excused amount.

Key Terms
القدر المعفو عنه (al-qadr al-maʿfū ʿanhu): The excusable amount of impurity (e.g., less than a dirham for liquid impurities).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) When the spread occurs after the prayer is completed
B) When the prayer is performed before the spread
C) When the oil is from a permissible source
D) When the spread is less than a dirham

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the validity of prayer with a spreading impurity depends on the amount at the start of prayer, not on subsequent spread.

Impurity Transfer from Wet to Dry Cloth: The Drip Criterion
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the deciding factor for impurity transfer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on impurity transfer from a wet impure cloth to a dry cloth, distinguish the positions of al-Halwani and other mashayikh, and apply the mufta bihi criterion.

Core Idea
Impurity transfers only if the wet impure cloth drips when squeezed — the source's moisture matters, not the receiver's absorbency.

In daily life, a wet impure garment may come into contact with a dry pure cloth. Does the dry cloth become impure? The answer depends on whether liquid can be expressed from the impure source. This passage reports an intra-Hanafi dispute over the decisive factor: al-Halwani focused on the dry cloth's own ability to drip, but the relied-upon view considers only the wet impure cloth's drip. Understanding this ruling prevents unnecessary declarations of impurity and eases practice.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling
Identify the ruling in the matn: a dry cloth wrapped in a wet impure cloth does not become impure if the wet cloth does not drip when squeezed.
This establishes the general principle that impurity transfer requires expressible moisture.
2
Identify the dispute
Recognize al-Halwani's position: he held that if the dry cloth itself would not drip when squeezed (due to absorbency), impurity does not transfer. The other mashayikh disagreed.
The dispute is about which cloth's ability to drip determines the ruling.
3
Analyze al-Halwani's argument
Al-Halwani based his view on the assumption that only a dry cloth that can itself drip would absorb enough impurity to transmit it. He considered this the 'correct' view.
This approach focuses on the receiving cloth's capacity.
4
Evaluate the mufta bihi view
The commentator (Tahtawi) rejects al-Halwani's view, citing observed reality: when washing, impurity spreads through the dry cloth without the cloth dripping. Thus, the criterion is the impure cloth's drip.
Empirical observation overrides theoretical consistency here.
5
Apply the ruling
Use the mufta bihi criterion: if the wet impure cloth drips when squeezed, the dry cloth becomes impure; otherwise not.
Ignore the dry cloth's absorbency; only the source matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما لا ينجس ثوب جاف طاهر لف في ثوب نجس رطب لا يتعصر الرطب لو عصر لعدم النجاسة إليه واختلف المشايخ فيما لو كان الثوب الجاف الطاهر بحيث لو لا يقطر فذكر الحلواني أنه لا ينجس في الأصح وفيه نظر لأن كثيراً من النجاسة ينتشر به الجاف ولا يقطر بالعصر كما هو مشاهد عند ابتداء غسله فلا يكون المتصل إليه مجرد نداوة إلا إذا كان النجس لا يقطر بالعصر فيتعين أن يفتى بخلاف ما صحح الحلواني
And similarly, a dry pure cloth wrapped in a wet impure cloth does not become impure if the wet cloth does not drip when squeezed, because no impurity transfers to it. The mashayikh differed regarding the case where the dry pure cloth is such that if [??] it does not drip. Al-Halwani mentioned that in the correct view it does not become impure, but there is consideration because much of the impurity spreads through the dry cloth and does not drip when squeezed, as is observed at the beginning of washing it, so what reaches it is not mere moisture unless the impure cloth does not drip when squeezed, so it is determined that fatwa be given contrary to what al-Halwani corrected.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about impurity transfer from wet impure cloth to dry cloth. The matn states that a dry cloth does not become impure if wrapped in a wet impure cloth that does not drip when squeezed. The commentator (Tahtawi) then reports an ikhtilaf among later Hanafi scholars (mashayikh) regarding a finer point: if the dry cloth itself is absorbent such that it would not drip if squeezed (i.e., it cannot be wrung out), does impurity still transfer? Al-Halwani held that it does not. But the commentator notes a problem: in practice, when washing such a cloth, impurity often spreads without the cloth dripping, meaning the moisture reaching the dry cloth is not mere water but contains impurity. Therefore, the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that the criterion is the impure cloth: if the impure cloth drips when squeezed, impurity transfers; otherwise it does not. This opposes al-Halwani's position. The authority weight is from later fatwa compilations.

Key Terms
يتعصر (yata'asṡar): To be squeezed such that liquid drips out.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi view in this passage, when does a dry cloth become impure if wrapped in a wet impure cloth?
A) If the dry cloth is absorbent and would not drip when squeezed.
B) If the wet impure cloth drips when squeezed.
C) Always, since any contact transfers impurity.
D) Only if a visible trace of impurity appears on the dry cloth.

Key Takeaway
The deciding factor for impurity transfer is whether the wet impure cloth drips when squeezed, not the absorbency of the dry cloth.

Wet Cloth on Dry Impure Ground
Ruling statement on impurity transfer from dry surface

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a wet cloth on dry impure ground and explain the condition that determines impurity transfer.

Core Idea
A wet cloth remains pure on dry impure ground unless a trace of impurity appears.

This lesson addresses a common scenario: a wet cloth is placed on ground that is impure but dry. The natural concern is whether the cloth becomes impure. The ruling provides clarity based on the principle that impurity is only transmitted when a visible trace or sufficient moisture is present. Understanding this helps avoid unnecessary purification and strengthens your grasp of impurity transfer rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is spreading a wet cloth on dry impure ground. The ruling is that the cloth does not become impure; it remains pure.
The ruling is explicitly stated: 'la yanjus' (does not become impure).
2
Identify the key condition
The condition is that the ground is 'yābisa' (dry). This is crucial because impurity transfer requires a moist medium.
Dry ground cannot transmit impurity through contact alone.
3
Understand the underlying principle
Impurity is determined by visible trace (like color, smell, taste) or by the flow of wetness from impurity. Here, no trace appears on the cloth, and the ground is dry, so no transfer occurs.
The cloth may wet the ground, but that does not reverse the transfer.
4
Apply the principle to similar cases
Consider: if the ground were wet, the cloth would become impure. If the cloth shows a trace of impurity, it becomes impure. This ruling applies only when both conditions are met.
The ruling is specific to dry ground and no visible trace.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ينجس ثوب رطب بنشره على أرض نجسة ببول أو سرقين لكنها يابسة فتندت الأرض منه أي من الثوب الرطب ولم يظهر أثرها فيه
And a wet cloth does not become impure by spreading it on ground impure with urine or dung, but dry, so the ground becomes moistened from it, i.e., from the wet cloth, and no trace of it appears in the cloth.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn. The commentator explains that if a wet cloth is spread on dry impure ground (e.g., soiled with urine or dry animal dung), the ground may become moist from the cloth but the cloth does not become impure because no trace of the impurity (like color or odor) transfers to it. The ruling is based on the principle that impurity is determined by visible trace or likely transfer; here, the ground is dry, so the impurity does not migrate to the cloth.

Key Terms
سرقين (sirqīn): Dried animal dung, considered najis (impure) in the Hanafi school.
بول (baul): Urine, considered najis (impure).
أرض نجسة (ard najisa): Ground that is impure due to contact with najasa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The cloth is impure because the ground is impure.
B) The cloth remains pure.
C) The cloth must be washed three times.
D) The ground becomes pure.

Key Takeaway
A wet cloth on dry impure ground remains pure unless a trace of impurity appears on the cloth.

Impurity from Wind on Cloth
Ruling on impurity via wind

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether wind carrying impurity makes a cloth impure and identify the condition that triggers impurity according to the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
Wind does not carry impurity to a cloth unless a visible trace appears.

This passage addresses a common concern: if wind blows over a dry impurity and then hits your cloth, does it become impure? The ruling is grounded in the principle that impurity is only transferred through direct contact or a visible trace. The Hanafi school emphasizes the necessity of a clear sign, which protects against unnecessary doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage considers wind that blows over a source of impurity (e.g., dried blood) and then strikes a cloth.
Assume the impurity is dry unless specified otherwise.
2
State the default ruling
The default ruling is that the cloth does NOT become impure. Impurity transfer requires more than air contact.
This is the relied-upon Hanafi position.
3
Identify the exception
If a visible trace of the impurity (color, odor, or wetness) appears on the cloth, then it becomes impure.
The trace must be actually observable; mere suspicion is not enough.
4
Note the secondary opinion
Some scholars (not the default) say that if the cloth itself is wet, the wind may directly transfer impurity through moisture contact, making it impure even without a trace.
The relied-upon position is the first: no impurity without trace.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ينجس الثوب بريح هبت على نجاسة فأصابت الريح الثوب إلا أن يظهر أثرها فيه أي الثوب وقيل ينجس إن كان مبلولاً لاتصالها به
And a cloth does not become impure by wind that blows over impurity and then hits the cloth unless a trace of it appears in the cloth. And it is said that it becomes impure if the cloth is wet because of contact with it.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This is a ruling from the matn with a secondary opinion. The basic ruling: wind carrying impurity (e.g., dust from dried blood) does not impurify a cloth it touches unless there is a visible trace (color or odor). However, some scholars say that if the cloth is wet, the wind may directly contact it with moisture, thus transferring impurity. The Hanafi relied-upon position is the first: no impurity without visible trace, even if the cloth is wet, because the wind is considered a 'arid (passing) and not a direct carrier of substantive impurity unless trace confirms.

Key Terms
ريح (rīḥ): wind that may carry microscopic particles of impurity
أثر (athar): trace, evidence (e.g., color, odor, or wetness) of impurity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) No, because no trace appears.
C) Yes, but only if the cloth was wet.
D) No, unless the wind was strong.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that wind does not transfer impurity to a cloth unless a visible trace of that impurity appears on it.

Air from Anus with Wet Seat: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on impurity transfer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two positions in the intra-Hanafi dispute on exiting air with a wet seat and identify which position is mufta bihi and why.

Core Idea
Exiting air is pure; wet seat does not make it impure.

This short passage captures a subtle intra-Hanafi disagreement: does the air that exits from the anus carry impurity if the anal area is wet? Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani held that it does, but the commentator asserts that the established correct view – and the mufta bihi – is that the air itself is pure, so wet clothing remains pure. This lesson will clarify the reasoning behind the majority position and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two rulings
Read the passage and note: the scenario is air exiting while the seat (anal area) is wet. Two rulings are mentioned: one by Shams al-A'imma (impurity), and the majority view (purity).
2
Examine the basis for the minority view
Consider why Shams al'A'imma might have ruled impurity. The text does not give his proof, but it likely involves the idea that the wetness could carry internal impurities.
His view is not relied upon in fatwa.
3
Understand the majority proof
The commentator refers to a previous established ruling: the purity of exiting air. This principle overrides any concern about the wet seat.
The phrase 'taqaddama anna saheeh tahaarah al-reeh al-khaarijah' indicates the dominant view.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and its authority
The commentator explicitly endorses the purity view. This is the position relied upon in fatwa by later Hanafis.
It is from the fatawa mutakhkhirin layer.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
If a person passes wind while his private area is wet, his clothes remain pure because the air is pure. No washing is required.

Source Text

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether the air that exits from the anus (fart) transfers impurity if the seat (the outlet) is wet. Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani held that it becomes impure (presumably because the wetness might carry particles). But the commentator states that the correct established position (which preceded) is that the air itself is pure, so even if the seat is wet, the clothing remains pure. This is the relied-upon view: exiting air is not a carrier of impurity.

Purification of Visible Impurity by Removal
Principle of purification by removal of visible substance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that visible impurity (e.g., blood) is purified by removal of its substance, one wash suffices, and residual trace is excused if removal is difficult.

Core Idea
Remove the substance, remove the impurity; one wash suffices.

In the Hanafi school, visible impurities (like blood) are treated differently from non-visible ones. The key principle is that purification is achieved by physically removing the impurity substance itself. This lesson explores that rule, including the intra-Hanafi discussion on the number of washes and the important exception that lingering colour or odour does not invalidate purification if it is difficult to remove.

Guided Path
1
Identify visible vs non-visible impurity
Determine if the impurity is visible ('ayniyya) like blood, or non-visible (hukmiyya). This classification determines the purification method.
Visible impurity is one whose substance can be seen and physically removed.
2
Apply the removal rule
For visible impurity, once the substance is removed (by washing, wiping, etc.), the item becomes pure. Repetition is not required on the correct view.
The text states 'bi zawaal 'aynihā' - by removal of its substance.
3
Note intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on wash count
Some Hanafi scholars (Abu Ja'far, Fakhr al-Islam) required additional washes by qiyas, but the relied-upon position is one wash.
The commentator affirms 'ala al-sahih' (on the correct view).
4
Understand the trace exception
If after removal a trace (colour/odour) remains that can only be removed with non-water agents (e.g., soap, alkali), it is excused and does not negate purity.
The key is difficulty (mashaqqah); water alone cannot remove it.
5
Apply to examples
Examples: dyed cloth becomes pure when wash water runs clear, impure oil trace excused, but carrion fat is itself impurity.
Distinguish between impurity substance and trace.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويطهر متنجس سواء كان بدناً أو ثوباً أو آنية بنجاسة ولو غليظة مرئية كدم بزوال عينها ولو كان بمرة أي غسلة واحدة على الصحيح ولا يشترط التكرار لأن النجاسة فيه باعتبار عينها فتزول بزوالها وعن الفقيه أبي جعفر أنه يغسل مرتين بعد زوال العين إلحاقاً لها بغير مرئية غسلت مرة وعن فخر الإسلام ثلاثاً بعده كغير مرئية لم تغسل، ومسح محل الحجامة بثلاث خرق رطبات نظاف مجزىء عن الغسل لأنه يعمل عمله ولا يضر بقاء أثر كلون أو ريح في محلها شق زواله والمشقة أن يحتاج في إزالته لغير الماء، أو غير المائع كحرض، وصابون لأن الآلة المعدة لتطهير الماء فالثوب المصبوغ بمتنجس يطهر إذا صار الماء صافياً مع بقاء اللون وقيل : يغسل بعده ثلاثاً ولا يضر أثر دهن متنجس على الأصح لزوال النجاسة المجاورة بالغسل بخلاف شحم الميتة لأنه عين النجاسة
And an impure thing is purified, whether body, garment, or vessel, from impurity even if thick, visible, like blood, by removal of its substance, even if it is with one time, i.e., one wash, according to the correct view, and repetition is not required because impurity is considered by its substance, so it is removed by its removal. And from al-Faqih Abu Ja'far that he washes twice after removal of the substance, analogizing it to non-visible impurity that was washed once; and from Fakhr al-Islam three times after it, like non-visible impurity that was not washed. And wiping the place of cupping with three clean wet cloths suffices for washing because it accomplishes its effect. And the remaining trace like color or odor in its place does not harm if its removal is difficult, and difficulty means that it needs something other than water for removal, such as alkali and soap, because the tool prepared for purification is water. So a garment dyed with an impure dye is purified when the water becomes clear despite remaining color; and it is said that it is washed after it three times. And the trace of impure oil does not harm in the correct view because the adjacent impurity is removed by washing, unlike carrion fat because it is the substance of impurity.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This is a major ruling on taharah: purification from visible impurity (like blood) is achieved simply by removing the substance itself, even with one wash. The correct Hanafi view does not require multiple washes for visible impurity; the substance's removal suffices. The commentator then mentions two dissenting opinions: Abu Ja'far al-Hinduwani required two washes after removal (by qiyas with non-visible impurity that had one wash), and Fakhr al-Islam required three (by qiyas with non-visible that had not been washed). The relied-upon view remains that one wash is sufficient. The rule of 'trace does not harm' is then elaborated: if after washing, a stain (color) or odor remains that would require non-water agents to remove, it is excused because the function of purification is accomplished by water. Examples follow: a garment dyed with an impure dye becomes pure when the runoff water is clear, even if color remains; impure oil's trace is excused because the fat itself is not present; carrion fat is different because it is itself the impure substance.

Key Terms
النجاسة المرئية (al-najāsah al-mariyyah): Visible impurity, such as blood, that can be seen and removed physically.
الغسلة الواحدة (al-ghaslah al-wāḥidah): One wash; for visible impurity, one complete wash that removes the substance suffices.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Blood spills on a garment and is washed once; the stain remains but the water runs clear. Is the garment pure according to the Hanafi school?
A) No, because the stain remains.
B) Yes, because the substance is removed.
C) No, because visible impurity requires three washes.
D) Yes, but only if the stain is not visible.

Key Takeaway
Visible impurity is purified by removing its substance; one wash suffices and residual color/odor that is hard to remove does not affect purity.

Purification of Specific Impure Substances
Rulings on purifying impure substances

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi methods for purifying specific impure substances (oil, honey, pottery, wood, polished vessels, and cooked meat) and identify which substances can be purified and which become permanently impure.

Core Idea
Meat cooked with impurity can never be purified; other substances require specific methods such as washing, boiling, scraping, or wiping.

This passage details the Hanafi rulings on purifying various substances that have become impure. While liquids like oil and honey can be purified by specific water treatments, solids like pottery and wood require washing or scraping depending on their age. The most critical ruling is that meat which has been cooked with impurity (e.g., in impure oil) becomes permanently impure and cannot be purified, even by boiling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the substance and its state
The passage covers ghee/oil, honey, pottery (new vs old), polished vessels, wood (new vs old), and cooked meat/chicken.
Distinguish between new and old pottery/wood — the method differs.
2
Recall the purification method for each
For each substance, there is a specific procedure: washing/decanting for oil, boiling for honey, multiple washings or burning for pottery, wiping for polished vessels, scraping for new wood and washing for old wood, and permanently impure for meat.
Note the number of repetitions: three times for oil and honey.
3
Note the ikhtilaf on meat
Abu Yusuf allowed boiling three times with pure water, but the mufta bihi follows Abu Hanifa that meat cooked with impurity never becomes pure.
The difference arises from whether impurity fully penetrates the meat.
4
Apply to the chicken example
The same rule applies to chicken boiled before its innards are removed — it is permanently impure.
The passage mentions scalding for plucking; the ruling remains the same.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسمن والدهن المتنجس يطهر الماء عليه ورفعه عنه ثلاثاً والعسل يصب عليه الماء ويغليه حتى يعود كما كان ثلاثاً والفخار الجديد يغسل ثلاثاً بانقطاع تقاطره في كل منها، وقيل: يحرق الجديد ويغسل القديم والأواني الصقيلة تظهر بالمسح والخشب الجديد ينحت والقديم يغسل واللحم المطبوخ بنجس حتى نضج لا يطهر، وقيل : يغلى ثلاثاً بالماء الطاهر، ومرقته تصب لا خير فيها وعلى هذا الدجاج المغلي قبل إخراج إمعائها، وأما وضعها بقدر انحلال المسام لنتف
And impure ghee and oil are purified by pouring water on them and lifting it off, three times. And honey: water is poured on it and boiled until it returns as it was, three times. And new pottery is washed three times, with each time stopping its dripping; and it is said: the new is burned, and the old is washed. And polished vessels are purified by wiping. And new wood is scraped, and old is washed. And meat cooked with impurity until done does not become pure; and it is said: it is boiled three times with pure water, and its broth is poured away, there is no good in it. And on this is the chicken boiled before removing its innards; and as for putting it to the extent of loosening pores for plucking.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents specific rulings for purifying various substances that have become impure. For liquid fats (ghee, oil), purification involves pouring water over them and decanting the water, repeated three times. Honey requires adding water and boiling until it returns to its original consistency, three times. For pottery: new earthenware, where impurity may have penetrated the pores, requires three washes until water no longer drips; some say burning is needed for new, while old (glazed) pottery is simply washed. Polished metal vessels are purified by wiping. Wood: new wood must be scraped (because impurity penetrates), old wood is washed (since it is sealed). Meat cooked with impurity is a complicated case: the majority say it never becomes pure; a minority (attributed to Abu Yusuf) allows boiling three times with water, discarding the broth. The same principle applies to chicken that is boiled before its innards are removed; I think this refers to the process of scalding for plucking. The relied-upon Hanafi position is that meat cooked with impurity is permanently impure and cannot be purified.

Key Terms
الحفر (al-ḥifr): Earthenware (pottery); new pottery may require burning, old only washing.
اللحم المطبوخ بنجس (al-laḥm al-maṭbūkh bi-najas): Meat cooked with an impure substance (e.g., wine, impure oil).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A chicken is boiled in water that was impure before its innards were removed. According to the mufta bihi, what is the ruling?
A) It is pure if boiled three times.
B) It is permanently impure.
C) It becomes pure if the water is poured away.
D) It is pure if the chicken was alive.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prescribes specific purification methods for various impure substances, but meat cooked with impurity is an exception — it becomes permanently impure and cannot be purified.

Purification of Cloth and Oil: Traces, Difficulty, and Methods
Commentary explaining rulings on traces, difficulty, and purification methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on traces of impurity (athar) when removal involves hardship, describe the purification methods for impure oil and fat, and explain why soap becomes pure by washing its surface.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, a difficult-to-remove trace is forgiven; impure oil is purified by water separation, solid fat by cutting, and soap by washing.

This lesson combines two key Hanafi rulings: first, the principle of hardship (mashaqqah) that allows forgiveness of traces (athar) of impurity, with applications to dyed garments and oil stains. Second, the practical methods for purifying impure oils, fats, and soap. These rules demonstrate the school's balance between avoiding unnecessary hardship and ensuring ritual purity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle of hardship (mashaqqah)
Understand the core ruling: if removing a trace of impurity requires heating water to a boil (i.e., great hardship), it is not obligatory. Cold water suffices even if the trace remains.
This is based on the Shari'ah principle of lifting hardship (raf' al-haraj).
2
Apply to the dyed garment
A garment dyed with impure water becomes pure once the water runs clear during washing. The dye color that remains is considered a trace (athar) that is forgiven.
The binding of the dye to the cloth does not retain impurity.
3
Apply to the trace of impure oil
If impure oil stains a garment, after washing, the trace (color or slight residue) does not harm. The oil's actual impurity is removed by washing, unlike carrion fat which is itself impure.
The 'correct view' (al-asahh) in the Hanafi school is that such traces are excused.
4
Derive the ruling for soap
From the same principle, soap that becomes impure is purified by washing its surface. The adjacent impurity is removed, and the rest remains pure. Some non-Hanafi scholars disagree.
Soap is not considered porous in a way that traps impurity permanently.
5
Learn the methods for purifying oils and fats
For liquid impure oil: place it in a perforated vessel, pour water, stir, let the oil rise, then open the hole to drain the water. Repeat thrice. For solid fat: cut out the part that absorbed the impurity.
The method relies on the physical separation of the impurity via water or excision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (والمشقة الخ) أفاد في النهر أن الأثر إذا توقف زواله على تسخين الماء: وغليه، لا يلزمه ذلك ويكتفى بالبارد وإن بقي الأثر. قوله : (فالثوب المصبوغ الخ) تفريع على المصنف. قوله: (ولا يضر أثر دهن متنجس على الأصح من هذا الفرع يعلم حكم الصابون إذا تنجس فإنه إذا غسل زالت النجاسة المجاورة وبقي طاهراً وقال بعض العلماء من غير أهل المذهب أنه لا يطهر أبداً قوله : ورفعه عنه (ثلاثاً) أو يوضع في إناء مثقوب، ثم يصب عليه الماء فيعلو الدهن ويحركه، ثم يفتح الثقب إلى أن يذهب الماء، وهذا إذا كان مائعاً وأما إذا كان جامداً فيقوّر.
His saying: (and difficulty etc.) Al-Nahr mentioned that if the removal of the trace depends on heating water and boiling it, he is not obliged to do that, and cold water suffices even if the trace remains. His saying: (the garment dyed... etc.) is a branching off from the author. His saying: (and the trace of impure oil does not harm on the correct view) from this subsection the ruling of soap is known: if it becomes impure, then when washed, the adjacent impurity is removed and it remains pure. And some scholars from outside the madhhab say it never becomes pure. His saying: (and removing it three times) or place it in a perforated vessel, then pour water over it so the oil rises, then stir it, then open the hole until the water goes out. This is if it is liquid; if it is solid, then cut it out.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiyah explains the ruling regarding the difficulty of removing traces of impurity. The main ruling is that if removing a trace (athar) requires heating water to a boil and thus great hardship, it is not obligatory; one may suffice with cold water even if the trace remains. This is based on the principle that the Shari'ah does not impose hardship. Then the commentary addresses the case of a garment dyed with impure water. According to the sound view, the garment becomes pure once the water runs clear, even if the dye color remains. Some hold that it must be washed three times thereafter. The commentary then discusses the trace of impure oil or fat. The dominant view is that such a trace does not render the garment impure because the actual impurity (the oil's moist part) was removed by washing. This is unlike carrion fat, which is itself impure. The passage then provides practical methods for purifying impure oil or ghee: for liquid oil, one may pour water over it, then let it stand so the oil rises, then open a hole in the bottom of the pot to drain the water, repeating three times. Alternatively, use a perforated vessel. For solid fat, one cuts out the part that absorbed the impurity. The commentary also derives the ruling for soap: if it becomes impure, washing its surface suffices to purify it, contrary to some non-Hanafi scholars who claim it can never become pure.

Key Terms
أثر (athar): A trace or residue left after removal of the actual impurity, such as color or smell.
مشقة (mashaqqah): Hardship or difficulty in removing the trace, such as needing to heat water.
صابون (sabun): Soap, which if it becomes impure, can be purified by washing the surface.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi ruling on the trace of impure oil on a garment after washing?
A) The trace renders the garment impure and it must be discarded.
B) The trace does not harm, and the garment is pure on the correct view.
C) The trace must be removed with hot water.
D) The garment must be washed three more times.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, traces of impurity that are difficult to remove are excused, and impure oils and soaps can be purified through simple methods like washing or cutting.

Purification of Honey and Various Utensils in Hanafi Fiqh
Hāshiyah commentary on purifying honey, new and old earthenware, and wooden utensils

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for purifying honey, earthenware, wooden, and metal utensils, and distinguish the method based on material and age.

Core Idea
Purification method depends on material and age: new porous items need burning/scraping; old non-porous need washing; polished metal needs wiping.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi rules for purifying various utensils and honey. The commentary from Al-Tahtawi refines earlier rulings, showing that the method of purification is not uniform but tailored to the material's porosity and condition. Understanding these distinctions prevents incorrect purification that could leave impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the item and its category
Determine whether the item is honey, earthenware (new or old), wood (new or old), or metal/glass (polished or rough).
The passage covers honey, pottery, wood, and metal/glass.
2
Determine material and condition
Assess porosity: new earthenware and new wood are porous; old earthenware and old wood are non-porous due to use; polished metal/glass is non-porous; rough metal/glass is porous.
Old earthenware may behave like new if it dries after use (absorbs moisture).
3
Apply the corresponding purification method
From the four methods (burn, scrape, wipe, wash), select the correct one: new earthenware: burn or wash until clear; old earthenware: wash; new wood: scrape; old wood: wash; polished metal/glass: wipe; rough metal/glass: wash; honey: pour water and boil.
For honey, water is poured and boiled three times to return to original consistency.
4
Note the ikhtilaf on new earthenware
Two valid opinions: burning (from al-Nawazil) or washing until no drip (from al-Hawi). The commentator says both are acceptable.
No contradiction; both are valid ways.
5
Verify the ruling for special cases
Old earthenware that becomes dry after contamination is treated like new (may require burning). This is from al-Kamal's comment.
This prevents underestimating absorption when dry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والعسل) مثله الدبس كما في الشرح قوله: (يصب عليه الماء) أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان الماء قدره أولاً وبعضهم قيده بالأول قوله: (وقيل يحرق الجديد) ذكره في النوازل، وذكر الأول صاحب الحاوي. قال بعض الأفاضل: ولا مناقضة بينهما لأنهما طريقان للتطهير قوله ويغسل القديم أي يطهر بالغسل ثلاثاً جفف أو لا لأن النجاسة على ظاهره فقط فصار كالبدن قال الكمال ينبغي تقييد القديم بما إذا كان رطباً وقت تنجسه أما لو ترك بعد الإستعمال حتى جف فهو كالجديد لأنه يشاهد اجتذابه الرطوبة وفي البحر عن الحاوي القدسي الأواني ثلاثة أنواع خزف وخشب ،وحديد ونحوها وتطهيرها على أربعة أوجه حرق ونحت ومسح، وغسل، فإذا كان الإناء من خزف، أو حجر، أو كان جديداً ودخلت النجاسة في أجزائه يحرق وإن كان عتيقاً يغسل وإن كان من خشب، وكان جديداً ينحت وإن كان قديماً يغسل وإن كان من حديد، أو صفر، أو رصاص، أو زجاج، وكان صقيلاً . وإن كان خشناً يغسل اهـ من السيد.
His saying: (and honey) similar to it is molasses as in the commentary. His saying: (water is poured over it) he made it absolute, so it includes whether the water is sufficient at first or not, and some restricted it to the first. His saying: (and it is said: the new [earthenware] is burned) he mentioned it in al-Nawazil, and the first opinion is mentioned by Sahib al-Hawi. Some senior scholars said: there is no contradiction between them because they are two ways of purification. His saying: (and the old [earthenware] is washed) meaning it is purified by washing three times, whether dry or not, because the impurity is only on its surface, so it is like the body. Al-Kamal said: it should be restricted to the old one being moist at the time of its contamination; but if it was left after use until it dried, it is like the new because it is seen to absorb moisture. And in al-Bahr from al-Hawi al-Qudsi: utensils are of three types: pottery, wood, and iron and the like. Their purification is in four ways: burning, scraping, wiping, and washing. So if the utensil is of pottery or stone, or it is new and impurity has entered its parts, it is burned. If it is old, it is washed. If it is of wood and is new, it is scraped; if old, it is washed. If it is of iron, brass, lead, or glass and is polished, it is wiped; if rough, it is washed. Ended from al-Sayyid.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on purifying various items. First, honey is purified by pouring water over it and boiling until it returns to its original consistency, repeating three times. The same ruling applies to molasses (dibs). The water may be poured initially in a sufficient amount or not, but some restrict it to the initial amount. For earthenware vessels, the commentary presents two opinions: new earthenware should be burned in fire to purify it, or washed until no more drips appear. Old earthenware is simply washed three times because impurity only sticks to the surface. The commentary notes that old earthenware must be moist at the time of contamination; if it dried, it absorbs impurity like new pottery. For wooden utensils, new ones are scraped (to remove the contaminated layer), old ones are washed. For metal and glass utensils (iron, brass, lead, glass), if polished, they are purified by wiping (since non-porous); if rough, they are washed. This classification is from al-Hawi al-Qudsi. The passage illustrates the Hanafi method of tailoring purification methods to the material and porosity of the object.

Key Terms
قديم (qadim): Old (utensil) that has been used and become non-porous due to use, so impurity only on surface.
جديد (jadid): New (utensil) that is porous and absorbs impurity.
نحت (naht): Scraping the surface of new wood to remove the contaminated layer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How is a new earthenware pot that became impure purified according to the opinions cited?
A) It is washed three times only.
B) It is burned in fire or washed until dripping stops.
C) It is scraped.
D) It is wiped with a cloth.

Key Takeaway
Purification of utensils in Hanafi fiqh is methodical: the correct method depends on the material's porosity, which changes with age and use.

Hanafi Ruling on Meat and Chicken Cooked with Impurity
Hāshiyah commentary on ruling for meat and chicken cooked with impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that meat cooked with impurity until done never becomes pure, explain the ikhtilaf with Abu Yusuf, and describe the case of chicken thrown into boiling water.

Core Idea
Once meat is fully cooked with impurity, it is permanently impure; a chicken thrown into boiling water before gutting can be purified by washing.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies the absolute ruling for meat cooked with impurity: it never becomes pure. The commentator also addresses a weak narration from Abu Yusuf suggesting boiling could purify, and confirms the fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's view. The same principle is analogized to the case of a chicken plunged into boiling water to pluck feathers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling
The meat cooked until fully done (nuduj) does not become pure, ever.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf's reported view that boiling could purify is rejected; fatwa follows Abu Hanifa.
3
Apply to chicken case
A chicken thrown into boiling water before gutting, if the water reached boiling and the chicken remained until feathers loosen, then washing afterward purifies it.
4
Understand the legal reasoning
The boiling water opens pores and releases impurity, then washing completes purification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( حتى نضج لا يطهر) أي أبداً قوله (وقيل يغلى : قول أبي يوسف والفتوى على أنه لا يطهر أبداً، وهو قول أبي حنيفة ذكره الشرح فيما إذا طبخت الحنطة بخمر. قوله : (وعلى هذا الدجاج الخ) يعني لو ألقيت دجاجة حال غليان الماء قبل أن يشق بطنها لتنتف، أو كرش قيل أن يغسل إن وصل الماء إلى حد الغليان ومكثت فيه بعد ريشها فتطهر بالغسل وتمويه الحديد بعد سقيه بالنجس مرات ويتجه مرة لحرقه وقبل التمويه يطهر ظاهرها بالغسل ثلاثاً، والتمويه يطهر باطنها عند أبي يوسف وعليه الفتوى
His saying: (until cooked, it does not become pure) meaning ever. His saying: (and it is said: it is boiled) is the statement of Abu Yusuf, and the fatwa is that it never becomes pure, and it is the statement of Abu Hanifa, as mentioned in the commentary regarding wheat cooked with wine. His saying: (and upon this the chicken etc.) means if a chicken is thrown into boiling water before its belly is slit to pluck, or tripe, it is said that it should be washed if the water reached the boiling point and it remained there after its feathers, so it becomes pure by washing. And the tamyih (coating) of iron after it has been coated with impure water several times, and one time heating is sufficient for burning it. And before tamyih, its surface is purified by washing three times, and tamyih purifies its interior according to Abu Yusuf, and upon it is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage discusses the purification of food items that have been cooked with impurity. The ruling for meat that has been cooked with impurity until it is fully done (nuduj) is that it never becomes pure, by consensus of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, although a weak narration reports that Abu Yusuf considered boiling three times as purifying, but the fatwa is on the first view. Similarly, for a chicken that is plunged into boiling water before its innards are removed (to pluck feathers), if the water reaches boiling and the chicken remains until the feathers loosen, then washing the chicken afterward purifies it. This is because the boiling water expands the pores and releases the impurity, and then washing completes the process. The passage then addresses iron tools (like knives) that have been coated with impure water (tamyih). The ruling: the tool should be heated in fire until red-hot, then quenched in pure water, and this tamyih process should be repeated three times. Abu Yusuf holds that this purifies the interior as well, and the fatwa is on this view. Before tamyih, the surface can be purified by washing three times. The commentary notes that one time of heating is sufficient if the fire completely burns off the impurity, but the threefold process is to remove doubt.

Key Terms
نضج (nuduj): Being fully cooked; once meat is cooked with impurity to this state, it can never be purified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the relied-upon ruling (fatwa) for meat cooked with impurity until well-done?
A) It becomes pure after boiling with water three separate times.
B) It never becomes pure under any circumstances.
C) It can be purified if the broth is discarded and the meat washed.
D) It is permissible to eat if the impurity was minor.

Key Takeaway
Meat or chicken cooked with impurity until fully done is permanently impure and cannot be purified, even by boiling.

Tamyih of Iron Tools
Hāshiyah commentary on tamyih of iron

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the process of tamyih for purifying iron tools from impurity, and state the relied-upon position of Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Tamyih (heating and quenching) purifies iron tools both inside and outside, according to the fatwa.

This part of the hashiya explains a specific method for purifying iron tools that have absorbed impurity. The process, called tamyih, involves heating the tool until red-hot and then quenching it in pure water. According to Abu Yusuf, this purifies the interior as well, and the fatwa is on his view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
An iron tool has been contaminated with impurity that seeped into the metal.
2
Explain the pre-purification
Before tamyih, the surface can be purified by washing three times.
3
Describe the tamyih process
The tool is heated until red-hot (one time is sufficient for burning impurity) and then quenched in pure water, repeated three times.
4
Determine the efficacy
According to Abu Yusuf, tamyih purifies the interior as well; the fatwa follows this.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa may have had a different view, but the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf.

His saying: (until cooked, it does not become pure) meaning ever. This passage discusses the purification of food items that have been cooked with impurity. The ruling for meat that has been cooked with impurity until it is fully done (nuduj) is that it never becomes pure, by consensus of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, although a weak narration reports that Abu Yusuf considered boiling three times as purifying, but the fatwa is on the first view. Key Terms
تمويه (tamyih): A process of coating iron tools with water and heating them to purify, typically by quenching in water after firing.

Taharah — Purification

6 min
Purification by Tawmiyah and Istihalah
Explains two purification methods and their reliance for fatwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tawmiyah and istihalah as purifiers, and identify the relied-upon positions (mufta bihi) within the school.

Core Idea
Tawmiyah purifies interiors; istihalah transforms impurity into purity — both are accepted for fatwa.

In this lesson, we examine two unique methods of purification recognized by the Hanafi school: tawmiyah (plating with water) and istihalah (complete transformation of substance). The text presents the positions of Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa, and clarifies which view is relied upon for fatwa. Understanding these rulings is essential for grasping the Hanafi approach to taharah through istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two purification methods
Read the text and locate the two methods: tawmiyah (plating) and istihalah (transformation). Understand what each entails.
Tawmiyah involves heating and quenching a contaminated iron knife; istihalah is a complete change of substance, e.g., dead animal to salt.
2
Analyze tawmiyah: process and dispute
Note that before plating, the exterior is washed three times. The dispute is whether plating purifies the interior. Abu Yusuf says yes, and this is the fatwa.
The text states 'wa 'alayhi al-fatwa' – upon this is the fatwa.
3
Analyze istihalah: examples and ikhtilaf
Istihalah purifies impure substances that completely transform, like a dead animal becoming salt or feces becoming ash. There is disagreement: Abu Yusuf rejects, but the relied-upon position accepts it.
The mufta bihi is from Imam Muhammad and a narration from Abu Hanifa.
4
Determine the mufta bihi for both
For tawmiyah, the mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's view (purifies interior). For istihalah, the mufta bihi is that it purifies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ريشها فتطهر بالغسل وتمويه الحديد بعد سقيه بالنجس مرات ويتجه مرة لحرقه وقبل التمويه يطهر ظاهرها بالغسل ثلاثاً، والتمويه يطهر باطنها عند أبي يوسف وعليه الفتوى والاستحالة تطهر الأعيان النجسة كالميتة إذا صارت ملحاً والعذرة تراباً أو رماداً كما سنذكره
Its feathers are purified by washing. The plating of iron after it has been drenched with najas (impurity) several times, and it is plausible [to suffice with] once due to burning. Before plating, its exterior is purified by washing three times. Plating purifies its interior according to Abu Yusuf, and upon this is the fatwa. Istihalah (transformation) purifies impure substances, like a dead animal when it becomes salt, and feces when they become earth or ash, as we will mention.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of rulings on purification by tawmiyah (plating) and istihalah (transformation). The knife, after being contaminated, is plated with water three times (or once if burned). Before plating, the exterior is washed. The interior is purified by plating according to Abu Yusuf, which is the relied-upon position. Istihalah, i.e., a complete transformation of the substance, purifies; for example, a dead animal becoming salt or feces becoming ash. This principle is from the Hanafi school, with Abu Hanifa accepting istihalah (in one narration) and Abu Yusuf rejecting it.

Key Terms
التمويه (al-tawmiyah): Plating or coating an object, such as a knife, with water by heating and then quenching, to remove impurity.
الاستحالة (al-istihalah): Transformation of a substance from an impure state to a pure one, e.g., dead animal becoming salt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the fatwa regarding the purification of the interior of a knife through tawmiyah?
A) It is not purified even after tawmiyah.
B) It is purified only after washing three times before tawmiyah.
C) It is purified by tawmiyah according to Abu Yusuf.
D) It is purified only if the knife is burned.

Key Takeaway
Tawmiyah purifies interiors according to Abu Yusuf; istihalah purifies through complete transformation — both are mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.

Taharah — Purification

Purification of Actual Impurity with Water and Other Liquids
Ruling on removal of actual impurity with liquids

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on removing actual impurity from garments and body using water (absolute and used) and other pure liquids, and identify the exception for oil and milk.

Core Idea
Actual impurity is removed by water or any pure liquid that removes it, but oil and milk do not purify because they do not extract the impurity.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position on the liquids that can purify actual impurity (najāsah ḥaqīqiyyah). While water is the primary purifier, the school also allows certain other liquids under specific conditions. However, there is an important distinction between liquids that remove impurity (muzīl) and those that do not, such as oil and milk. We will also see the intra-Hanafi difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the use of oily substances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the impurity type
Distinguish actual impurity (ḥissī) from legal impurity (ḥukmī). This lesson deals only with actual impurity on garments and body.
2
State the primary purifier
Absolute water (māʾ muṭlaq) is the primary purifier by consensus. Used water (mustaʿmal) is also valid on the correct Hanafi view.
Shāfiʿīs do not allow used water for actual impurity.
3
Determine which other liquids are valid
Any pure liquid that has the property of removal (muzīl) purifies, e.g., vinegar and rose water. The condition is that it must remove the physical impurity.
The liquid must be ṭāhir (pure), not najis.
4
Identify exceptions
Oil and milk do not purify because they do not extract the impurity; they only spread it. A narration from Abū Yūsuf allows oil if the trace disappears, but this is not the relied-upon view.
The correct view (al-ṣaḥīḥ) excludes oil and milk.
5
Apply the distinction from ḥadath
Legal impurity (ḥadath) is only removed by water on explicit textual evidence; the ease for actual impurity does not extend to ḥadath.
Ḥadath requires wuḍūʾ or ghusl with water only.
