The doubt in its extraction in the apparent narration (Zāhir al-Riwāyah) and in one narration: is content with wringing once, and it is more appropriate. And placing it in flowing water suffices from three times and wringing, like a vessel; if he places it in it, it becomes filled and water comes out, it becomes pure. And if he washes it in vessels, they and the waters differ; the first vessel purifies, and what touches it by washing three times; the second by two; the third by one. And if he forgets the place of impurity and washes a part of the garment without research, its purity is ruled upon according to the chosen opinion, but if it appears in another place, he repeats the prayer. And the actual impurity, whether visible or invisible, is removed from the garment and body with absolute water (māʾ mutlaq) by agreement, and with used water (mustaʿmal) according to the correct view due to the strength of removal by it. Likewise, it is removed from the garment and body in the correct view with every pure liquid that removes, due to the existence of removal by it. So it is not removed with oil because it does not come out by itself, nor with milk, even if churned, in the correct view. And it is narrated from Abū Yūsuf: if he washes blood from a garment with oil, clarified butter (samn), or olive oil (zayt) until its trace goes, it is permissible. And the remover includes vinegar and rose water extracted from plants, due to the strength of its removal of the minute parts of impurity like water, contrary to ḥadath because it is ḥukmī (legal) and is specified with water by textual evidence (naṣṣ). And it is the easiest existing thing, so there is no hardship. And the breast becomes pure if the child suckles it and it has become impure by vomit three times with its saliva, and the mouth of the wine drinker by repeating its saliva and swallowing it and licking the finger three times from impurity. And Muḥammad specified purification with water, and it is one of two narrations from Abū Yūsuf. And the khuff and similar such as the sandal become pure with water and with liquid, and (by rubbing) with earth or soil from impurity that has mass, even if acquired from something else according to the correct view, like dust or ash placed on the khuff before its drying from liquid impurity, even if the mass is from its origin or by acquisition of mass from something else, (wet) according to the chosen view for fatwā, and most mashāyikh are upon it, due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'When one of you steps on impurity with his khuffs, their purification is with earth.' And due to his statement: 'When one of you comes to the mosque, let him look; if he sees on his sandals impurity or dirt, let him wipe them and pray in them.' It is restricted to the khuff to exclude the garment and carpet, and to exclude the body except for manī as previously mentioned. And the sword and similar, such as the mirror, oiled vessels, wood, kharāʾiṭ (?) [؟], ebony (ābnūs), and nail, become pure by wiping (al-masḥ) with dust or a cloth, because the parts of impurity do not penetrate them, or with the wool of a slaughtered sheep. So after wiping, only a little remains, which is not considered mass. If it is long, it is immersed in the intoxicant. His statement: (and his swallowing) has no restrictor. His statement: (and licking the finger three times) means that with its saliva repeated in it after the first time three times, and after the second time two times, and his mouth becomes pure after the third time one time, on the analogy of what came before regarding washing an impure thing in a vessel. His statement: (And the khuff and similar become pure) means with the condition that the trace goes away unless it is difficult. His statement: (and by rubbing) – Imam Muḥammad explicitly stated in al-Jāmiʿ that if he rubs it or removes what is dry, it becomes pure. The mashāyikh said: Were it not for what is in al-Jāmiʿ, we would stipulate wiping with dust, because it has an effect in purification. His statement: (from impurity that has mass) – the distinction between one with mass and other is that what is seen after drying, like feces and blood, has mass, and what is not seen does not, as in al-Tabyīn. And he restricted it to exclude one without mass, for it is washed by agreement, because moisture has entered its parts, and there is no attractant to it on its surface, so it does not come out except by washing. Manī is of those with mass, as mentioned by al-ʿAynī. His statement: (according to the chosen opinion for fatwā) – and Imam stipulated dryness, since wiping increases wetness and does not purify it. His statement: (al-adhā) means impure; he applied it to it because it is harmful, so it is applying the verbal noun intending the active participle. His statement: (fa-ṭuhūruhumā al-turāb) – with fatḥa on the ṭāʾ so that the predicate is correct. His statement: (aw qadharān) – its meaning, apparently, is disliked other than impure, like mucus. His statement: (wa yuṣallī fīhimā) – evidence for the recommendation of prayer in pure sandals, and it is stipulated in the madhhab. His statement: (exclusion of the garment) – it does not become pure by rubbing because its parts are interwoven, so many of its parts penetrate it. His statement: (and exclusion of the body) – its softness and moisture prevent removal of impurity by rubbing. His statement: (except for manī) – it is removed by friction. His statement: (and similar) – every smooth thing that has no pores. So excluded by the first is iron if it has rust or engraving on it, for it is only removed by washing; and excluded by the second is a smooth garment due to the existence of pores. His statement: (and purification is achieved by wiping truly) – he pointed by it to the disagreement concerning the purification of smooth surfaces by wiping: some say it manifests, others say it reduces. The benefit of the disagreement appears in what the author mentioned, and this disagreement applies to manī when rubbed, the earth when dry, the hides of dead animals when tanned with a legal tanning, the well when its water dries up and then returns, and the baked bricks (ājurr) when laid.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage from the hāshiyah of al-Ṭaḥtāwī on Marāqī al-Falāḥ is a critical refinement (taḥqīq) of rulings regarding the removal of actual (ḥissī) impurity from garments, bodies, and objects. The author clarifies multiple points: 

1. **Wringing and number of washes**: The Zāhir al-Riwāyah says wringing is required each time; another narration from Muḥammad suffices with one wringing. Placing a garment in flowing water substitutes for three times and wringing, just as a vessel filled and overflowed becomes pure.

2. **Forgetting location of impurity**: If one washes a part of the garment without searching (taḥarrī), the garment is ruled pure according to the chosen opinion. But if the impurity later appears elsewhere, the prayer must be repeated.

3. **Liquids for purification**: Actual impurity is removed from cloth and body by absolute water by consensus, and by used water (mustaʿmal) on the correct view. It is also removed by any pure (ṭāhir) liquid that removes (muzīl) – like vinegar and rose water – but not by oil or milk (even churned). A narration from Abū Yūsuf allows oil, samn, or zayt if the trace disappears. Ḥadath (ritual impurity) is different; it is legal (ḥukmī) and specified to water by naṣṣ.

4. **Purification of breast and mouth**: A child's breast becomes pure after sucking three times with saliva if contaminated by vomit; similarly the mouth of a wine drinker by repeating saliva, swallowing, and licking the finger three times. Muḥammad restricts purification to water; one narration from Abū Yūsuf agrees.

5. **Khuff and similar**: Khuff (leather socks) and sandals are purified by water or liquid, and by rubbing (dalk) on earth or soil from impurity that has mass (jirm), even if the mass is acquired (like dust on liquid impurity). The preferred view for fatwā requires the impurity to be wet and the rubbing to be on dry soil. Proof: hadith: 'If one of you steps on impurity with his khuffs, their purification is earth.' Also: 'Let him wipe them and pray in them.' This excludes garments (because parts penetrate) and body except manī (which is removed by friction).

6. **Sword and smooth surfaces**: Sword, mirror, oiled vessels, wood, ebony, and nails are purified by wiping (masḥ) with dust or a cloth because impurity parts do not penetrate. If metal has rust or engraving, washing is required. A smooth cloth has pores and requires washing. The disagreement: some say wiping manifests purification (manifestation of purity), others say it only reduces impurity to an imperceptible amount; the practical effect appears in cases like manī rubbed dry, earth dried, hides tanned, well water returning, and baked bricks laid.

Key Terms
النجاسة الحقيقية (al-najāsah al-ḥaqīqiyyah): Actual physical impurity (like blood, urine) that can be removed by washing or other means.
الماء المطلق (al-māʾ al-muṭlaq): Pure water that is not used (mustaʿmal) and not changed by a pure substance; it is the primary purifier.
المائع المزيل (al-māʾiʿ al-muzīl): A pure liquid that has the property of removing physical impurity, e.g., vinegar or rose water.
الحدث (al-ḥadath): Legal impurity (ritual impurity) that is removed by wuḍūʾ or ghusl with water only.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafī school, which of the following liquids is NOT valid for removing a bloodstain from a garment?
A) Absolute water (māʾ muṭlaq)
B) Used water (mustaʿmal)
C) Vinegar (khall)
D) Olive oil (zayt)

Key Takeaway
Actual impurity is removed by water or any pure liquid that removes it; oil and milk do not purify because they lack the property of removal.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
12 min
Purification of Khuff and Similar Items by Rubbing (Dalk)
Ruling on purification of khuff by rubbing (dalk)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that khuff (leather socks) and similar items can be purified from impurity with mass by rubbing on dry earth, citing the hadith and conditions.

Core Idea
Khuff can be purified by rubbing on dry earth for wet impurity with mass; hadith supports the ease.

This lesson focuses on a specific ease granted by the Hanafī school: the purification of khuff (leather socks) and similar items (like sandals) from impurity that has mass (jirm). Instead of washing, one can rub them on dry earth or a similar surface. The ruling is based on prophetic hadith and has conditions regarding the type of impurity and the state of the khuff.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object and impurity type
The object is the khuff or similar (e.g., sandal, shoe). The impurity must have mass (jirm) – visible after drying – and be wet. The chosen view for fatwā requires the impurity to be wet.
Impurities without mass (e.g., thin liquids) require washing.
2
Apply the method of rubbing (dalk)
Rub the khuff on dry earth or soil until the trace of impurity is gone. The hadith says: 'When one of you steps on impurity with his khuffs, their purification is with earth.'
The earth must be dry; rubbing on wet earth does not work.
3
Consider exceptions
This ruling excludes garments (because parts penetrate) and the body (except manī). Garments require washing; body rubbing is not effective except for manī.
Manī is exceptional because it can be removed by friction.
4
Note the condition of trace removal
The impurity must be removed such that no trace remains (or only an imperceptible amount). If it is difficult to remove completely, the ruling still applies as long as the effort is made.
5
Evaluate the proof
The ruling is based on ahad hadith. The Hanafīs accept it as evidence for dalk, while other schools (Shāfiʿī) may require water.
The dalāla is zannī, but the school acts on it.

Source Text

تظهر بدهن لعدم خروجه بنفسه ولا باللبن، ولو مخيضاً في الصحيح، وروي عن أبي يوسف : لو غسل الدم من الثوب بدهن أو سمن أو زيت حتى ذهب أثره جاز والمزيل كالخل وماء الورد المستخرج من البقول لقوة إزالته لأجزاء النجاسة المتناهية كالماء بخلاف الحدث لأنه حكمي وخص بالماء بالنص، وهو أهون موجود فلا حرج ويطهر الثدي إذا رضعه الولد وقد تنجس بالقيء ثلاث مرات بريقه وقم شارب الخمر بترديد ريقه وبلعه ولحس الإصبع ثلاثاً عن نجاسة وخص التطهير محمد بالماء، وهو إحدى الروايتين عن أبي يوسف ويطهر الخف ونحوه كالنعل بالماء وبالمائع و(بالدلك) بالأرض أو التراب (من نجاسة لها جرم) ولو مكتسباً من غيرها على الصحيح كتراب أو رماد وضع على الخف قبل جفافه من نجاسة مائعة ولو كانت المتجسدة من أصلها أو باكتساب الجرم من غيرها (رطبة) على المختار للفتوى وعليه أكثر المشايخ لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : إذا وطىء أحدكم الأذى بخفيه فظهورهما التراب ولقوله : إذا جاء أحدكم المسجد فلينظر فإن رأى في نعليه أذى، أو قذراً فليمسحهما وليصل فيهما قيد بالخف احترازاً عن الثوب والبساط و احترازاً عن البدن إلا في المني لما تقدم ويطهر السيف ونحوه) كالمرآة والأواني المدهونة، والخشب الخرائط والآبنوس والظفر (بالمسح) بتراب أو خرقة لأنها لا تتداخلها أجزاء النجاسة، أو صوف الشاة المذبوحة، فلا يبقى بعد المسح إلا القليل وهو غير معتبر جرم، إذا كان طويلاً إنغمس في المسكر قوله : ( وبلعه) ليس له محترز. قوله: (ولحس الأصبع ثلاثاً) أي مع تردد ريقه فيه بعد الأولى ثلاثاً وبعد الثانية مرتين ويطهر فمه بعد الثالثة بمرة على قياس ما تقدم فيما إذا غسل النجس في إجانة. قوله : ويطهر الخف ونحوه) أي بشرط ذهاب الأثر إلا ان يشق قوله : (وبالدلك) صرح الإمام محمد في الجامع بأنه لو حكه أوحت ما يبس طهر قال المشايخ لولا ما في الجامع لشرطنا المسح بالتراب لأن له أثراً في الطهارة. قوله : (من نجاسة لها (جرم الفاصل بين ذي الجرم وغيره أن ما يرى بعد الجفاف كالعذرة، والدم ذو ومالاً فلا كذا في التبيين، واحترز به عن غير ذي الجرم فإنه يغسل إتفاقاً لأن البلل دخل في أجزائه، ولا جاذب له في ظاهره فلا يخرج إلا بالغسل والمني من ذي الجرم ذكره العيني. قوله : (على المختار للفتوى وشرط الإمام الجفاف إذ المسح يكثر الرطب، ولا يطهره. قوله : (الأذى) أي النجس أطلقه عليه لأنه يؤذي فهو من إطلاق المصدر وإرادة إسم الفاعل قوله: (فطهورهما التراب) بفتح الطاء ليصح الأخبار قوله: (أو قذراً) المراد به فيما يظهر المستقذر غير النجس كنحو مخاط. قوله (وليصل فيهما) دليل على استحباب الصلاة في النعال الطاهرة، وهو منصوص عليه في المذهب. قوله : (إحترازاً عن الثوب) فلا يطهر بالدلك لأن أجزاءه متخللة فيتداخله كثير من أجزائها قوله (واحترازاً عن البدن) فإن لينه ورطوبته تمنع من إخراج النجاسة بالدلك قوله : (إلا) في المني) فإنه يظهر بالفرك. قوله: (ونحوه) من كل صقيل لا مسام له فخرج بالأول الحديد إذا كان عليه صدأ أو منقوشاً، فإنه لا يظهر إلا بالغسل وخرج بالثاني الثوب الصقيل لوجود المسام. قوله : ( ويحصل بالمسح حقيقة التطهير الخ) أشار به إلى الخلاف في طهارة الصقيل بالمسح ، فقيل مظهر، وقيل مقلل، وفائدة الخلاف تظهر فيما ذكره المصنف وهذا الخلاف يجري في المني إذا فرك، والأرض إذا جفت، وجلود الميتة إذا دبغت دباغة حكمية، والبئر إذا غارت، ثم عاد ماؤها والآجر المفروش إذا
The doubt in its extraction in the apparent narration (Zāhir al-Riwāyah) and in one narration: is content with wringing once, and it is more appropriate. And placing it in flowing water suffices from three times and wringing, like a vessel; if he places it in it, it becomes filled and water comes out, it becomes pure. And if he washes it in vessels, they and the waters differ; the first vessel purifies, and what touches it by washing three times; the second by two; the third by one. And if he forgets the place of impurity and washes a part of the garment without research, its purity is ruled upon according to the chosen opinion, but if it appears in another place, he repeats the prayer. And the actual impurity, whether visible or invisible, is removed from the garment and body with absolute water (māʾ mutlaq) by agreement, and with used water (mustaʿmal) according to the correct view due to the strength of removal by it. Likewise, it is removed from the garment and body in the correct view with every pure liquid that removes, due to the existence of removal by it. So it is not removed with oil because it does not come out by itself, nor with milk, even if churned, in the correct view. And it is narrated from Abū Yūsuf: if he washes blood from a garment with oil, clarified butter (samn), or olive oil (zayt) until its trace goes, it is permissible. And the remover includes vinegar and rose water extracted from plants, due to the strength of its removal of the minute parts of impurity like water, contrary to ḥadath because it is ḥukmī (legal) and is specified with water by textual evidence (naṣṣ). And it is the easiest existing thing, so there is no hardship. And the breast becomes pure if the child suckles it and it has become impure by vomit three times with its saliva, and the mouth of the wine drinker by repeating its saliva and swallowing it and licking the finger three times from impurity. And Muḥammad specified purification with water, and it is one of two narrations from Abū Yūsuf. And the khuff and similar such as the sandal become pure with water and with liquid, and (by rubbing) with earth or soil from impurity that has mass, even if acquired from something else according to the correct view, like dust or ash placed on the khuff before its drying from liquid impurity, even if the mass is from its origin or by acquisition of mass from something else, (wet) according to the chosen view for fatwā, and most mashāyikh are upon it, due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'When one of you steps on impurity with his khuffs, their purification is with earth.' And due to his statement: 'When one of you comes to the mosque, let him look; if he sees on his sandals impurity or dirt, let him wipe them and pray in them.' It is restricted to the khuff to exclude the garment and carpet, and to exclude the body except for manī as previously mentioned. And the sword and similar, such as the mirror, oiled vessels, wood, kharāʾiṭ (?) [؟], ebony (ābnūs), and nail, become pure by wiping (al-masḥ) with dust or a cloth, because the parts of impurity do not penetrate them, or with the wool of a slaughtered sheep. So after wiping, only a little remains, which is not considered mass. If it is long, it is immersed in the intoxicant. His statement: (and his swallowing) has no restrictor. His statement: (and licking the finger three times) means that with its saliva repeated in it after the first time three times, and after the second time two times, and his mouth becomes pure after the third time one time, on the analogy of what came before regarding washing an impure thing in a vessel. His statement: (And the khuff and similar become pure) means with the condition that the trace goes away unless it is difficult. His statement: (and by rubbing) – Imam Muḥammad explicitly stated in al-Jāmiʿ that if he rubs it or removes what is dry, it becomes pure. The mashāyikh said: Were it not for what is in al-Jāmiʿ, we would stipulate wiping with dust, because it has an effect in purification. His statement: (from impurity that has mass) – the distinction between one with mass and other is that what is seen after drying, like feces and blood, has mass, and what is not seen does not, as in al-Tabyīn. And he restricted it to exclude one without mass, for it is washed by agreement, because moisture has entered its parts, and there is no attractant to it on its surface, so it does not come out except by washing. Manī is of those with mass, as mentioned by al-ʿAynī. His statement: (according to the chosen opinion for fatwā) – and Imam stipulated dryness, since wiping increases wetness and does not purify it. His statement: (al-adhā) means impure; he applied it to it because it is harmful, so it is applying the verbal noun intending the active participle. His statement: (fa-ṭuhūruhumā al-turāb) – with fatḥa on the ṭāʾ so that the predicate is correct. His statement: (aw qadharān) – its meaning, apparently, is disliked other than impure, like mucus. His statement: (wa yuṣallī fīhimā) – evidence for the recommendation of prayer in pure sandals, and it is stipulated in the madhhab. His statement: (exclusion of the garment) – it does not become pure by rubbing because its parts are interwoven, so many of its parts penetrate it. His statement: (and exclusion of the body) – its softness and moisture prevent removal of impurity by rubbing. His statement: (except for manī) – it is removed by friction. His statement: (and similar) – every smooth thing that has no pores. So excluded by the first is iron if it has rust or engraving on it, for it is only removed by washing; and excluded by the second is a smooth garment due to the existence of pores. His statement: (and purification is achieved by wiping truly) – he pointed by it to the disagreement concerning the purification of smooth surfaces by wiping: some say it manifests, others say it reduces. The benefit of the disagreement appears in what the author mentioned, and this disagreement applies to manī when rubbed, the earth when dry, the hides of dead animals when tanned with a legal tanning, the well when its water dries up and then returns, and the baked bricks (ājurr) when laid.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage from the hāshiyah of al-Ṭaḥtāwī on Marāqī al-Falāḥ is a critical refinement (taḥqīq) of rulings regarding the removal of actual (ḥissī) impurity from garments, bodies, and objects. The author clarifies multiple points: 

1. **Wringing and number of washes**: The Zāhir al-Riwāyah says wringing is required each time; another narration from Muḥammad suffices with one wringing. Placing a garment in flowing water substitutes for three times and wringing, just as a vessel filled and overflowed becomes pure.

2. **Forgetting location of impurity**: If one washes a part of the garment without searching (taḥarrī), the garment is ruled pure according to the chosen opinion. But if the impurity later appears elsewhere, the prayer must be repeated.

3. **Liquids for purification**: Actual impurity is removed from cloth and body by absolute water by consensus, and by used water (mustaʿmal) on the correct view. It is also removed by any pure (ṭāhir) liquid that removes (muzīl) – like vinegar and rose water – but not by oil or milk (even churned). A narration from Abū Yūsuf allows oil, samn, or zayt if the trace disappears. Ḥadath (ritual impurity) is different; it is legal (ḥukmī) and specified to water by naṣṣ.

4. **Purification of breast and mouth**: A child's breast becomes pure after sucking three times with saliva if contaminated by vomit; similarly the mouth of a wine drinker by repeating saliva, swallowing, and licking the finger three times. Muḥammad restricts purification to water; one narration from Abū Yūsuf agrees.

5. **Khuff and similar**: Khuff (leather socks) and sandals are purified by water or liquid, and by rubbing (dalk) on earth or soil from impurity that has mass (jirm), even if the mass is acquired (like dust on liquid impurity). The preferred view for fatwā requires the impurity to be wet and the rubbing to be on dry soil. Proof: hadith: 'If one of you steps on impurity with his khuffs, their purification is earth.' Also: 'Let him wipe them and pray in them.' This excludes garments (because parts penetrate) and body except manī (which is removed by friction).

6. **Sword and smooth surfaces**: Sword, mirror, oiled vessels, wood, ebony, and nails are purified by wiping (masḥ) with dust or a cloth because impurity parts do not penetrate. If metal has rust or engraving, washing is required. A smooth cloth has pores and requires washing. The disagreement: some say wiping manifests purification (manifestation of purity), others say it only reduces impurity to an imperceptible amount; the practical effect appears in cases like manī rubbed dry, earth dried, hides tanned, well water returning, and baked bricks laid.

Key Terms
الخف (al-khuff): Leather socks worn over the foot, commonly used in the time of the Prophet and still used in some cultures.
الدلك (al-dalk): Rubbing with earth or a dry substance to remove impurity from khuff or smooth surfaces.
الجرم (al-jirm): The physical mass or residue of an impurity that remains after drying; distinguishes between types of impurities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view for fatwā, what condition must the impurity on the khuff satisfy for rubbing to be valid?
A) It must be dry.
B) It must be wet.
C) It must be from a liquid source.
D) It must be acquired from another object.

Key Takeaway
Khuff and similar smooth items can be purified from wet impurity with mass by rubbing on dry earth, based on prophetic hadith and scholarly consensus.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
10 min
Purification of Smooth Surfaces (Sword, Mirror) by Wiping (Masḥ)
Ruling on purification of smooth surfaces by wiping (masḥ)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that smooth surfaces (e.g., swords, mirrors) are purified by wiping (masḥ) with a dry substance, and evaluate the dispute over whether wiping manifests or reduces impurity.

Core Idea
Smooth surfaces are purified by wiping with dust or cloth because impurity does not penetrate; only wiping is needed.

In this lesson, we explore the purification of objects that are non-porous and smooth, such as swords, mirrors, oiled vessels, and even nails. Because impurity cannot penetrate these surfaces, the school allows purification by simple wiping (masḥ) with a dry substance like dust or a cloth. There is a subtle disagreement among scholars about whether wiping truly manifests purity (iẓhār) or merely reduces impurity to an imperceptible level (taqlīl). This dispute has practical consequences in related cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category of objects
Smooth surfaces without pores: sword, mirror, oiled vessels, wood like ebony, and even fingernails. The key is that impurity does not penetrate.
Iron with rust or engraving is excluded; it requires washing.
2
State the method: wiping (masḥ)
Wipe the surface with dust, a cloth, or even the wool of a slaughtered sheep until the impurity is removed. Only a negligible trace may remain.
The object must be truly smooth; smooth cloth is not included because it has pores.
3
Understand the dispute: manifestation vs. reduction
Some scholars say wiping manifests purity (iẓhār) – the object becomes fully pure. Others say it only reduces impurity to a negligible amount (taqlīl) – the object is still technically impure but the small amount is excused.
The dispute extends to other cases: manī rubbed dry, dry earth, tanned hides, etc.
4
Note exceptions
Objects with pores (like iron with rust, fabric) require washing. The head of a nail is smooth but if set in wood, only the surface is smooth; the wood part may require washing if impurity seeps.
5
Apply to related cases
Similar rulings apply to drying earth (when impurity dries it purifies), tanning of hides (dabgh), and wells that dry up and refill.
These analogies come from the same principle of removing the source of impurity.

The doubt in its extraction in the apparent narration (Zāhir al-Riwāyah) and in one narration: is content with wringing once, and it is more appropriate. And placing it in flowing water suffices from three times and wringing, like a vessel; if he places it in it, it becomes filled and water comes out, it becomes pure. And the remover includes vinegar and rose water extracted from plants, due to the strength of its removal of the minute parts of impurity like water, contrary to ḥadath because it is ḥukmī (legal) and is specified with water by textual evidence (naṣṣ). And it is the easiest existing thing, so there is no hardship. And the breast becomes pure if the child suckles it and it has become impure by vomit three times with its saliva, and the mouth of the wine drinker by repeating its saliva and swallowing it and licking the finger three times from impurity. And Muḥammad specified purification with water, and it is one of two narrations from Abū Yūsuf. And the khuff and similar such as the sandal become pure with water and with liquid, and (by rubbing) with earth or soil from impurity that has mass, even if acquired from something else according to the correct view, like dust or ash placed on the khuff before its drying from liquid impurity, even if the mass is from its origin or by acquisition of mass from something else, (wet) according to the chosen view for fatwā, and most mashāyikh are upon it, due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'When one of you steps on impurity with his khuffs, their purification is with earth.' And due to his statement: 'When one of you comes to the mosque, let him look; if he sees on his sandals impurity or dirt, let him wipe them and pray in them.' It is restricted to the khuff to exclude the garment and carpet, and to exclude the body except for manī as previously mentioned. And the sword and similar, such as the mirror, oiled vessels, wood, kharāʾiṭ (?) [؟], ebony (ābnūs), and nail, become pure by wiping (al-masḥ) with dust or a cloth, because the parts of impurity do not penetrate them, or with the wool of a slaughtered sheep. So after wiping, only a little remains, which is not considered mass. If it is long, it is immersed in the intoxicant. His statement: (and his swallowing) has no restrictor. His statement: (and licking the finger three times) means that with its saliva repeated in it after the first time three times, and after the second time two times, and his mouth becomes pure after the third time one time, on the analogy of what came before regarding washing an impure thing in a vessel. His statement: (And the khuff and similar become pure) means with the condition that the trace goes away unless it is difficult. His statement: (and by rubbing) – Imam Muḥammad explicitly stated in al-Jāmiʿ that if he rubs it or removes what is dry, it becomes pure. The mashāyikh said: Were it not for what is in al-Jāmiʿ, we would stipulate wiping with dust, because it has an effect in purification. His statement: (from impurity that has mass) – the distinction between one with mass and other is that what is seen after drying, like feces and blood, has mass, and what is not seen does not, as in al-Tabyīn. And he restricted it to exclude one without mass, for it is washed by agreement, because moisture has entered its parts, and there is no attractant to it on its surface, so it does not come out except by washing. Manī is of those with mass, as mentioned by al-ʿAynī. His statement: (according to the chosen opinion for fatwā) – and Imam stipulated dryness, since wiping increases wetness and does not purify it. His statement: (al-adhā) means impure; he applied it to it because it is harmful, so it is applying the verbal noun intending the active participle. His statement: (fa-ṭuhūruhumā al-turāb) – with fatḥa on the ṭāʾ so that the predicate is correct. His statement: (aw qadharān) – its meaning, apparently, is disliked other than impure, like mucus. His statement: (wa yuṣallī fīhimā) – evidence for the recommendation of prayer in pure sandals, and it is stipulated in the madhhab. His statement: (exclusion of the garment) – it does not become pure by rubbing because its parts are interwoven, so many of its parts penetrate it. His statement: (and exclusion of the body) – its softness and moisture prevent removal of impurity by rubbing. His statement: (except for manī) – it is removed by friction. His statement: (and similar) – every smooth thing that has no pores. So excluded by the first is iron if it has rust or engraving on it, for it is only removed by washing; and excluded by the second is a smooth garment due to the existence of pores. His statement: (and purification is achieved by wiping truly) – he pointed by it to the disagreement concerning the purification of smooth surfaces by wiping: some say it manifests, others say it reduces. The benefit of the disagreement appears in what the author mentioned, and this disagreement applies to manī when rubbed, the earth when dry, the hides of dead animals when tanned with a legal tanning, the well when its water dries up and then returns, and the baked bricks (ājurr) when laid.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage from the hāshiyah of al-Ṭaḥtāwī on Marāqī al-Falāḥ is a critical refinement (taḥqīq) of rulings regarding the removal of actual (ḥissī) impurity from garments, bodies, and objects. The author clarifies multiple points: 

1. **Wringing and number of washes**: The Zāhir al-Riwāyah says wringing is required each time; another narration from Muḥammad suffices with one wringing. Placing a garment in flowing water substitutes for three times and wringing, just as a vessel filled and overflowed becomes pure.

2. **Forgetting location of impurity**: If one washes a part of the garment without searching (taḥarrī), the garment is ruled pure according to the chosen opinion. But if the impurity later appears elsewhere, the prayer must be repeated.

3. **Liquids for purification**: Actual impurity is removed from cloth and body by absolute water by consensus, and by used water (mustaʿmal) on the correct view. It is also removed by any pure (ṭāhir) liquid that removes (muzīl) – like vinegar and rose water – but not by oil or milk (even churned). A narration from Abū Yūsuf allows oil, samn, or zayt if the trace disappears. Ḥadath (ritual impurity) is different; it is legal (ḥukmī) and specified to water by naṣṣ.

4. **Purification of breast and mouth**: A child's breast becomes pure after sucking three times with saliva if contaminated by vomit; similarly the mouth of a wine drinker by repeating saliva, swallowing, and licking the finger three times. Muḥammad restricts purification to water; one narration from Abū Yūsuf agrees.

5. **Khuff and similar**: Khuff (leather socks) and sandals are purified by water or liquid, and by rubbing (dalk) on earth or soil from impurity that has mass (jirm), even if the mass is acquired (like dust on liquid impurity). The preferred view for fatwā requires the impurity to be wet and the rubbing to be on dry soil. Proof: hadith: 'If one of you steps on impurity with his khuffs, their purification is earth.' Also: 'Let him wipe them and pray in them.' This excludes garments (because parts penetrate) and body except manī (which is removed by friction).

6. **Sword and smooth surfaces**: Sword, mirror, oiled vessels, wood, ebony, and nails are purified by wiping (masḥ) with dust or a cloth because impurity parts do not penetrate. If metal has rust or engraving, washing is required. A smooth cloth has pores and requires washing. The disagreement: some say wiping manifests purification (manifestation of purity), others say it only reduces impurity to an imperceptible amount; the practical effect appears in cases like manī rubbed dry, earth dried, hides tanned, well water returning, and baked bricks laid.

Key Terms
المسح (al-masḥ): Wiping a smooth surface with a dry substance to remove impurity, sufficient for purification.
الصقيل (al-ṣaqīl): A smooth, polished surface without pores, such as a sword or mirror.
الإظهار والإقلال (al-iẓhār wa al-iqlāl): Two views on whether wiping manifests full purity or only reduces impurity to an excusable amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafī school, which of the following objects can be purified by dry wiping (masḥ)?
A) A wool sweater with dried blood
B) A stainless steel knife with oil
C) A wooden spoon with milk
D) A leather belt with wet mud

Key Takeaway
Smooth, non-porous surfaces are purified by dry wiping because impurity does not penetrate; the dispute over iẓhār vs. taqlīl does not affect the practical ruling.

Purification of Khuffs by Rubbing from Wet Najasah with Jirm
Ruling on purifying khuffs by rubbing with earth from wet najasah with jirm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purifying khuffs by rubbing with earth and apply the conditions of wetness and jirm to determine when this method is valid.

Core Idea
Wet najasah with a visible body can be removed from khuffs by rubbing with earth; dry or bodyless najasah requires washing.

How should a person purify their leather footwear after stepping on wet filth? The Hanafi school provides a practical concession: rubbing with earth suffices, but only when the najasah has a visible body and is wet. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its evidence from the Prophet's practice, and the important distinctions that limit its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that khuffs (and similar items like sandals) are purified by rubbing (dalk) with earth from wet najasah that has a body (jirm). This is a valid purification method in the Hanafi school.
The ruling applies specifically to khuffs and their analogues, not to garments or the body.
2
Examine the conditions for validity
The najasah must be wet and have a visible body at the time of rubbing. If the najasah is dry or has no body, rubbing does not suffice; washing is required. Also, the trace of najasah should be removed unless it is too difficult.
The condition of 'wetness' is critical; rubbing a dry stain does not purify.
3
Analyse the dalil (proof)
The supporting evidence is two hadith: one stating that stepping on filth purifies by soil, and another commanding to wipe sandals and pray in them. The Hanafi school interprets these as establishing permissibility of rubbing for purification.
The hadith are ahad, so they provide zanni thubut and dalala, but are relied upon due to practice of the Companions.
4
Note the exceptions
Garments and the body (except for semen) are not purified by rubbing because their absorbent nature prevents complete removal of najasah. Semen is an exception: it can be removed by rubbing even from the body.
Semen is considered to have a body and can be removed by friction.
5
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and mufta bihi
Imam Muhammad and Abu Hanifa hold that rubbing suffices; Abu Yusuf prefers washing. The mufta bihi is the former, as followed by most mashayikh. The Shafi'i school rejects rubbing and requires washing in all cases.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within and outside the school.

His saying: (and excluding the body) for its softness and moisture prevent the removal of najasah by rubbing. His saying: (except in semen) for it becomes apparent by rubbing.

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on purifying khuffs and similar items by rubbing (dalk) when they come into contact with wet najasah that has a visible body (jirm). The Hanafi school, following the chosen position for fatwa, states that rubbing with earth or soil purifies the khuff provided the najasah is wet and has a body. The proof is from two hadith: one instructs that stepping on filth with khuffs is purified by soil; the other encourages wiping sandals and praying in them. The commentary clarifies conditions: the khuff must be such that the trace can be removed by rubbing; if it is difficult, washing is not required. Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' holds that rubbing suffices, and the mashayikh note that if not for his authority, they would require soil due to its purifying effect. The ruling distinguishes between najasah with body (like feces) which can be removed by rubbing, and without body (like urine) which requires washing. Semen is an exception for the body, as it can be removed by rubbing.

Key Terms
الخف (al-khuff): Leather sock or boot worn over the foot, often used in purification rulings.
الدلك (al-dalk): Rubbing or friction, here specifically rubbing with earth or soil to remove najasah.
جرم (jirm): A visible body or solid substance of the najasah that remains after drying.

Khuffs and similar items are purified by rubbing with earth only when the najasah is wet and has a visible body; otherwise, washing is required.

Purification of Smooth Surfaces by Wiping: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on purifying smooth surfaces by wiping, with dispute on whether it truly purifies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on whether wiping smooth surfaces purifies or reduces najasah and identify the operative ruling (mufta bihi) for fatwa.

Core Idea
Wiping a smooth surface like a sword can purify it completely according to the chosen Hanafi view, based on the practice of the Companions.

This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya addresses a practical question: does wiping a sword (or any smooth, non-porous surface) actually purify it from najasah, or does it merely reduce the impurity? The answer affects everyday acts like eating food cut with such a sword. Let's explore the intra-Hanafi dispute and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Define Saqil and its Exclusions
Identify what qualifies as a smooth surface (saqil) – items without pores like swords, mirrors, and oiled vessels. Exclude rusty or engraved iron (porous) and smooth cloth (pores present).
The key characteristic: no pores that can absorb najasah.
2
Identify the Two Positions
Recognize the two views: tathir (wiping effects real purification) and taqlil (wiping only reduces najasah to a negligible amount). Note the main proponents: al-Isbijabi (tathir) and al-Quduri (taqlil).
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not between madhhabs.
3
Trace the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
If wiping purifies (tathir), food cut with the sword is halal. If only reduction (taqlil), the sword remains impure and food becomes impure.
This applies to all cases: wet/dry, urine/feces, etc.
4
Understand the Tarjih (Preference)
The majority of mashayikh and the chosen for fatwa (mukhtar) favor tathir. Evidence: the Companions wiped their swords after battle and prayed with them, indicating they considered them pure.
The taqlil view's objection (some najasah remains) is answered by deeming the trace negligible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ونحوه) من كل صقيل لا مسام له فخرج بالأول الحديد إذا كان عليه صدأ أو منقوشاً، فإنه لا يظهر إلا بالغسل وخرج بالثاني الثوب الصقيل لوجود المسام. قوله : ( ويحصل بالمسح حقيقة التطهير الخ) أشار به إلى الخلاف في طهارة الصقيل بالمسح ، فقيل مظهر، وقيل مقلل، وفائدة الخلاف تظهر فيما ذكره المصنف وهذا الخلاف يجري في المني إذا فرك، والأرض إذا جفت، وجلود الميتة إذا دبغت دباغة حكمية، والبئر إذا غارت، ثم عاد ماؤها والآجر المفروش إذا ويحصل بالمسح حقيقة التطهير في رواية، فإذا قطع بها البطيخ يحل أكله واختاره الاسبيجابي، ويحرم على رواية التقليل واختاره القدوري ولا فرق بين الرطب والجاف والبول والعذرة على المختار للفتوى لأنّ الصحابة رضي الله عنهم كانوا يقتلون الكفار بسيوفهم، ثم يمسحونها ويصلون معها
His saying: (and the like) meaning every smooth thing that has no pores. By the first, iron is excluded if it has rust or engraving, for it is not cleaned except by washing. By the second, smooth cloth is excluded due to the presence of pores. His saying: (and real purification is achieved by wiping) he pointed to the disagreement about the purity of smooth surfaces by wiping. Some say it is purifying, others say it is reducing. The benefit of the disagreement appears in what the author mentioned. And this disagreement applies to semen if rubbed, earth if dried, skins of dead animals if tanned with a valid tanning, a well if its water disappears then returns, and baked bricks if... And real purification is achieved by wiping in one narration. If one cuts watermelon with it, eating it is permissible, and al-Isbijabi chose that. It is prohibited according to the narration of reduction, and al-Quduri chose that. And there is no difference between wet and dry, urine and feces according to the chosen for fatwa, because the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, used to kill the disbelievers with their swords, then wipe them and pray with them.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage discusses the purification of swords and similarly smooth surfaces (saghil) that have no pores, such as mirrors, oiled vessels, and certain woods. The Hanafi ruling is that these are purified by wiping (mash) with soil or a dry cloth, unlike porous materials which require washing. The commentary then presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute: does wiping actually purify (haqiqat al-tathir) or merely reduce the najasah to a negligible amount (taqlil)? The practical consequence is whether food cut with such a sword becomes permissible to eat. Al-Isbijabi chose the former view, al-Quduri the latter. The chosen position for fatwa is that there is no difference between wet and dry najasah or between urine and feces, based on the practice of the Companions. This dispute also applies to other cases like semen rubbed off, earth dried, tanned hides, and wells.

Key Terms
الصقيل (al-saqil): A smooth, non-porous surface that does not absorb liquids.
المسح (al-mash): Wiping or passing the hand or a cloth over the surface to remove najasah.
التقليل (al-taqlil): The view that wiping only reduces the amount of najasah without fully purifying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Food cut with the sword is always impure.
C) The sword must be washed after every use.
D) There is no practical difference.

Key Takeaway
Within the Hanafi school, the preferred view is that wiping (mash) on smooth surfaces effects true purification (tathir), based on the practice of the Companions, making such surfaces legally pure.

Purification of Earth by Drying from Najasah
Ruling on earth becoming pure once it dries after najasah is removed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that earth becomes pure by drying after najasah removal, distinguish between tahir and tahur status, and apply the exception regarding tayammum.

Core Idea
Earth that dries after najasah becomes pure for praying but loses its purifying property for tayammum.

In this passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi, we examine the Hanafi view on purification of earth after najasah. The ruling is based on a hadith and distinguishes between the earth being pure (tahir) and being purifying (tahur). We'll explore the conditions, the proof, and the ikhtilaf regarding tayammum.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the purification of earth after the trace of najasah disappears and the earth dries. The ruling: the earth becomes pure (tahir), and prayer on it is permissible.
The ruling does not require the sun; drying by any means (wind, fire, shade) suffices according to the correct view.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith: 'Any land that has dried has become purified.' This is the primary evidence for purification by drying.
The hadith is found in Sunan Abi Dawud and is considered ahad (zanni al-thubut).
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'zakt' (purified) directly indicates that drying causes purification. The Hanafi principle: a general command or statement indicates the ruling unless specified otherwise.
The proof is strong enough for the ruling but not for tayammum because of additional texts requiring the earth to be 'tayyib' (purifying).
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There is a difference of opinion on whether tayammum can be performed with such earth. The preferred view (mufta bihi) is that it cannot, because the earth lacks the property of being a purifier (tahur). However, there is a weak narration allowing it.
The source mentions that the chosen view for fatwa is the prohibition of tayammum.
5
Understand the distinction tahir vs tahur
Before najasah, earth had both qualities: being pure (tahir) and being purifying (tahur). Najasah removes both. Drying restores only the purity (tahir), not the purifying property (tahur). Therefore, tayammum is invalid.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school and has practical implications for travelers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ذهب أثر النجاسة عن الأرض و) قد (جفت) ولو بغير الشمس على الصحيح ظهرت وجازت الصلاة عليها لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: (أيما أرض جفت فقد زكت) (دون التيمم منها في الأظهر لاشتراط الطيب نصاً وروي جوازه منها وبظهر ما بها أي الأرض من شجر وكلا) أي عشب (قائم) أي نابت فيها (بجفافه) من تنجس وجفت نجاسته، ثم قلع كذا في الشرح قوله : (واختاره الاسبيجابي) وهو الأولى بالاعتبار لإطلاق المتون ولا يخفى الاحتياط. قوله : ( على المختار للفتوى) وقيل طريقه أن يمسحه بثوب مبلول ذكره السيد أي يمسح النجس اليابس. قوله : ( وإذا ذهب أثر النجاسة عن الأرض) المراد بالأرض ما يشمله إسم الأرض كالحجر، والحصى، والآجر، واللبن، وريح، وظل وتقييد الهداية بالشمس ،اتفاقي، وإذا أراد تطهيرها عاجلاً فقيه تفصيل إن كانت رخوة تتشرب الماء فإنه يصب عليها الماء حتى يغلب على ظنه أنها طهرت ولا توقيت في ذلك، وإن كان صلبة إن كانت منحدرة حفر في أسفلها حفرة، وصب عليها الماء، فإذا اجتمع الماء في تلك الحفرة، كبسها أعني تلك الحفرة بالتراب وإن كانت مستوية صب عليها الماء ثلاث مرات، وجففت كل مرة بخرقة طاهرة، وكذا لو صب عليها الماء بكثرة حتى لا يظهر أثر النجاسة، وكذا لو قلبها بجعل الأعلى أسفل، وعكسه، أو كبسها بتراب ألقاه عليها فلم يوجد ريح . النجاسة طهرت قوله : لاشتراط الطيب نصاً وهو الطهور أي ولم يوجد وذلك لأنها قبل التنجس كان الثابت لها وصفين الطاهرية والطهورية فلما تنجست زال عنها الوصفان وبالجفاف ثبت لها الطاهرية وبقي
And if the trace of najasah goes away from the earth and it has dried, even by other than the sun according to the correct view, it becomes pure and prayer is permissible upon it, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Any land that has dried has become purified.', except for tayammum from it according to the most apparent view, due to the text requiring good (tayyib) earth. And it is narrated that it is permissible from it. And whatever is on it, i.e., the earth, of trees and grass, i.e., herbage, standing, i.e., growing in it, becomes pure by its drying if it became impure and its najasah dried, then it is cut. Such is in the sharh. His saying: (and al-Isbijabi chose it) and it is the first to consider due to the absolute nature of the texts, and caution is not hidden. His saying: (according to the chosen for fatwa) and it is said that its method is to wipe it with a moist cloth, mentioned by al-Sayyid, meaning he wipes the dry najas. His saying: (and if the trace of najasah goes away from the earth) meaning the earth that is included in the name of earth, such as stone, pebbles, baked bricks, sun-dried bricks, and the like when they are embedded in the earth not separate from it. If not, then washing is necessary, and it does not become pure by drying because then it is not called earth in custom. Therefore, it is not included in the sale of legally, due to lack of connection to it in a permanent manner. So it is not considered like it, as in al-Qahistani, Munyat al-Musalli and its two commentaries by al-Halabi and Ibn Amir Hajj, except that they made it absolute regarding pebbles and did not condition connection. And in al-Khaniyya: If the stone absorbs the najasah like the millstone, it becomes pure by drying like earth. If it does not absorb, meaning like marble, it does not become pure except by washing. Al-Halabi carried this detail to the separate stone that is moved and transferred, and the author of al-Durr followed him, saying: The separate is washed only, except a rough stone like a millstone, it is like earth. His saying: (and it has dried) It is said: jaffa al-thawb yajiffu with kasra jufufan, and yajuffu with fatha as a dialect, when it was wet and dried. If it has dew, then if it dries completely, it is said: qaffa. As in al-Sihah and others, and the meaning here is the second, as taken from what comes from al-Qahistani. His saying: (even if by other than the sun) like fire, wind, and shade, and the restriction of al-Hidayah to the sun is accidental. And if one wants to purify it quickly, there is a detail: If it is soft and absorbs water, then he pours water on it until he is sure that it is pure, and there is no time restriction. If it is hard: if it is sloping, he digs a pit at its bottom, pours water on it, and when the water collects in that pit, he packs it, i.e., that pit, with soil. If it is level, he pours water on it three times, and dries it each time with a pure cloth. Also, if he pours water on it profusely until no trace of najasah appears, or if he turns it by putting the top bottom, and vice versa, or packs it with soil thrown on it until no smell of najasah remains, it becomes pure. His saying: due to requiring good (tayyib) textually, meaning pure, and it is not found. That is because before becoming impure, it had two descriptions: purity (tahirah) and purifying (tahuria). When it became impure, both descriptions left. By drying, purity (tahirah) is established, and the purifying property remains? (incomplete).
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage discusses the purification of earth when najasah is removed and the earth dries. The hadith states: 'Any land that has dried has become purified.' The Hanafi ruling is that once the trace of najasah disappears and the ground dries, it is pure and salah can be performed upon it. However, tayammum cannot be performed with it because the earth must have the property of being a purifier (tahur), which is lost after najasah. The text details conditions: the earth must be that which is customarily called earth, i.e., fixed and not separate. Stones and pebbles that are embedded are included; separate ones require washing. The method of quick purification is also given: for soft absorbent earth, pour water until cleanliness is verified; for hard earth, either dig a pit to collect water and cover it, or pour water three times, or turn the soil, etc. The passage concludes by explaining that earth initially had two properties: being pure (tahir) and being purifying (tahur). Najasah removes both; drying restores purity but not the purifying property.

Key Terms
الجفاف (al-jafaf): Drying of the earth after najasah is removed, making it pure.
الطهورية (al-tahuriyya): The property of being a purifier, i.e., capable of being used for tayammum.
الطاهرية (al-tahiriyya): The property of being pure (ritually clean) in itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because it is pure
B) No, because it has lost its purifying (tahur) property
C) Yes, but only if it dried under the sun
D) No, because it is still considered impure

Key Takeaway
Drying after najasah removal purifies earth for prayer but does not restore its purifying property for tayammum.

Purification by Wiping and Its Effect on Food
Critical refinement on two narrations about wiping

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi narrations on purification by wiping and explain which is the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Wiping can achieve true purification – the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi tackles a practical issue: can a knife that was merely wiped after contact with impurity become pure enough to cut food? The Hanafi school has two narrations. We will examine the dispute, the evidence from the Companions' practice, and the ruling adopted for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The issue is whether wiping (masḥ) a najis object, such as a sword, achieves true purification (haqiqat al-tat hir) or only reduces impurity (taqlil). This determines whether a melon cut with a wiped knife becomes permissible to eat.
The ruling hinges on the nature of wiping as a purification method.
2
Recognize the two narrations
There are two positions within the Hanafi school: one holds that wiping achieves true purification (chosen by al-Asbijabi), the other holds that it only minimizes impurity (chosen by al-Quduri).
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that wiping does achieve true purification. Therefore, a melon cut with a wiped knife is permissible to eat. This view was selected by al-Asbijabi.
The author states 'على المختار للفتوى' – the chosen for fatwa.
4
Understand the scope of the ruling
The ruling applies equally to wet and dry impurities, and to both urine and stool. There is no distinction in this regard.
This broadens the application of the ruling.
5
Examine the proof (dalil)
The Companions (Sahaba) used to kill disbelievers with their swords, then wipe them and pray with them. Since prayer requires purity, this practice shows that wiping suffices for purification.
The proof is a Qawl Sahabi, strong in Hanafi methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحصل بالمسح حقيقة التطهير في رواية، فإذا قطع بها البطيخ يحل أكله واختاره الاسبيجابي، ويحرم على رواية التقليل واختاره القدوري ولا فرق بين الرطب والجاف والبول والعذرة على المختار للفتوى لأنّ الصحابة رضي الله عنهم كانوا يقتلون الكفار بسيوفهم، ثم يمسحونها ويصلون معها
True purification is achieved by wiping in one narration. So if one cuts a melon with it (a knife purified by wiping), eating it is permissible – and this was chosen by al-Asbijabi. And it is forbidden according to the narration of taqlil chosen by al-Quduri. And there is no difference between wet and dry, and between urine and stool according to the relied-upon position for fatwa, because the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, used to kill the disbelievers with their swords, then wipe them and pray with them.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on the Maraqi al-Falah. It addresses the ruling on purification achieved by merely wiping (masḥ) a najis object. The text presents two narrations within the Hanafi school: one holds that wiping achieves true purification (taṭhīr ḥaqīqī), so a knife used to cut a melon after being wiped makes the melon permissible to eat; the other holds that wiping only reduces impurity (taqlīl), making the melon forbidden. The relied-upon position for fatwa follows the first narration, as chosen by al-Asbijabi. The ruling applies equally to wet and dry impurities and to both urine and stool. The proof is the practice of the Companions, who would wipe their swords after killing enemies and then pray with them, indicating that wiping suffices.

Key Terms
المسح (al-masḥ): Wiping; a method of purification by rubbing without water, considered sufficient in certain contexts.
التقليل (al-taqlīl): The narration that wiping only minimizes impurity rather than removing it completely.
الاسبيجابي (al-Asbijābī): A Hanafi scholar who chose the view that wiping achieves true purification.
القدوري (al-Qudūrī): A Hanafi scholar who chose the view that wiping only reduces impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position for fatwa, what happens if a melon is cut with a knife that has been purified by wiping?
A) The melon becomes najis and cannot be eaten.
B) The melon is permissible to eat.
C) The melon becomes pure only if it is washed.
D) The knife must be washed before cutting the melon.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the mufta bihi is that wiping can achieve true purification, and food cut with a wiped knife is permissible.

Purification of Earth by Drying (Jafāf)
Critical refinement on purification of earth by drying with exception for tayammum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purification of earth by drying, identify its conditions, and explain why such earth cannot be used for tayammum.

Core Idea
Earth becomes pure by drying, but not purifying — tayammum requires tahur, not just tahir.

In this lesson, we examine a critical refinement in Hanafi taharah: the purification of earth through the process of drying (jafāf). While the disappearance of the impurity's trace and the earth's dryness render it pure for praying upon, a key limitation arises regarding tayammum. Understanding this distinction is essential for correct practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
State that the earth becomes pure when the trace of impurity disappears and the earth dries, regardless of the drying source (sun, wind, fire, shade).
The sound view does not require sunlight specifically.
2
Examine the proof
The hadith 'Any earth that dries has become purified' is cited as evidence. Note that it is a ahad hadith, accepted in Hanafi fiqh.
The hadith is explicit in using the word 'zakat' meaning purification.
3
Apply the exception for tayammum
Even though the earth becomes pure (tahir), it does not become purifying (tahur). Tayammum requires the earth to be tahur as per the nass (textual condition). Therefore, tayammum from such earth is not valid according to the dominant view.
There is a weak narration allowing tayammum, but the mufta bihi rejects it.
4
Extend to attached plants
Trees and grass that are growing in the earth also become pure by drying when they become najis and their own impurity dries. This applies as long as they are still attached; if cut after drying, they remain pure.
This follows from the same principle of jafāf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ذهب أثر النجاسة عن الأرض وقد جفت ولو بغير الشمس على الصحيح ظهرت وجازت الصلاة عليها لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: (أيما أرض جفت فقد زكت) دون التيمم منها في الأظهر لاشتراط الطيب نصاً وروي جواز منها وبظهر ما بها أي الأرض من شجر وكلا أي عشب قائم أي نابت فيها بجفافه من تنجس وجفت نجاسته، ثم قلع كذا في الشرح
And when the trace of impurity disappears from the earth and it has dried, even by other than the sun according to the sound view, it becomes pure and prayer is permissible upon it, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Any earth that dries has become purified.' Except for tayammum from it in the most manifest view, because purity of the soil is a condition textually. And it is reported that tayammum is permissible from it. And whatever is on it, i.e., the earth, such as trees and herbage, i.e., grass, standing, i.e., growing in it, become pure by its drying when they become najis and their impurity dries, then they are cut, as in the Sharh.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement on the purification of earth by the process of drying (jafāf). According to the soundest view, if impurity dries on the ground and its trace disappears, the ground becomes pure, and it is permissible to pray on it. This is supported by a hadith: 'Any earth that dries has become purified.' Drying can occur by sun, wind, fire, or shade. However, this purification does not extend to allowing tayammum with that earth, because tayammum requires the earth to be pure (tahir) and also purifying (tahur), and drying only restores the first quality. There is a narration that tayammum is permissible, but the dominant view is otherwise. Additionally, plants and trees growing in the earth are also purified by drying, as long as they are still attached; if they are cut after drying, they remain pure.

Key Terms
الجفاف (al-jafāf): Drying; the process by which moisture of impurity disappears, leading to purification of earth.
التيمم (al-tayammum): Dry ablution with earth; requires earth to be both pure and purifying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It must be washed with water three times.
B) It is purified by the disappearance of trace and drying, even without sun.
C) It becomes pure only if the sun dries it.
D) It can never be purified; the surface must be removed.

Key Takeaway
Earth becomes pure by drying and can be prayed upon, but it does not regain the purifying quality needed for tayammum.

What Counts as 'Earth' for Purification by Drying?
Critical refinement defining 'earth' for purification by drying

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which objects qualify as 'earth' for purification by drying and apply the Hanafi condition of attachment.

Core Idea
Purification by drying applies only to earth and its attached materials, not separate objects.

The Hanafi school permits purification of the earth by drying when the impurity's trace disappears. But what exactly is 'earth'? This lesson refines the definition: it includes fixed components like embedded stones and bricks, but excludes loose, separate objects unless they are absorbent like millstones. Understanding this distinction prevents invalid purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Recall that earth is purified by drying (jafaf) when the impurity's trace vanishes. The definition of 'earth' determines its scope.
The base ruling is from the earlier matn.
2
Recognize the condition of attachment
The passage states that stones, bricks, etc. are considered 'earth' only if they are embedded (mutedākhila) and fixed in the ground, not separate.
Attachment is key; custom ('urf) does not call separate objects 'earth'.
3
Note the exception for absorbent separate stones
Even a separate stone that absorbs impurity (like a millstone) is purified by drying, by analogy to earth. Non-absorbent stones (e.g., marble) require washing.
This is from al-Khaniyah and adopted by later authorities.
4
Consider the intra-Hanafi variant on pebbles
Some Hanafi scholars did not require connection for pebbles. Be aware of this difference, though the relied-upon view (muftā bihī) conditions attachment.
Al-Halabi and Ibn Amir Hajj note the absolute treatment of pebbles in some sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( وإذا ذهب أثر النجاسة عن الأرض) المراد بالأرض ما يشمله إسم الأرض كالحجر، والحصى، والآجر، واللبن، ونحوها إذا كانت متداخلة في الأرض غير منفصلة عنها وإن لم تكن كذلك فلا بد من الغسل، ولا تظهر بالجفاف لأنها حينئذ لا تسمى أرضاً عرفاً، ولذا لا تدخل في بيع الأرض حكماً لعدم اتصالها بها على جهة القرار، فلا تلحق بها كما في القهستاني، ومنية المصلي وشرحيها للحلبي وابن أمير حاج إلا انهم أطلقوا في الحصى، فلم يقيدوه بالاتصال، وفي الخانية : الحجر إذا كان يتشرب النجاسة كحجر الرحى يظهر بالجفاف كالأرض، وإن كان لا يتشرب يعني كالرخام لا يظهر إلا بالغسل، وحمل الحلبي هذا التفصيل في الحجر المنفصل الذي ينقل ويحول وعليه مشى صاحب الدر حيث قال : فالمنفصل يغسل لا غير إلا حجراً خشناً كرحى فكارض اهـ.
His saying: 'And when the trace of impurity disappears from the earth' – what is meant by 'earth' is that which the name 'earth' includes, such as stone, pebbles, brick, adobe, and the like, if they are embedded in the earth, not separate from it. And if they are not like that, then washing is necessary, and purification by drying is not effective, because then they are not called 'earth' customarily. Therefore, they are not included in the sale of land in legal rulings, due to lack of connection to it in a fixed manner. So they are not attached to it, as in al-Qahustani, Munyat al-Musalli, and its two commentaries by al-Halabi and Ibn Amir Hajj, except that they were absolute regarding pebbles and did not condition connection. In al-Khaniyah: Stone that absorbs impurity, like a millstone, is purified by drying like earth, and if it does not absorb, meaning like marble, it is only purified by washing. Al-Halabi interpreted this detail as applying to separate stones that are moved and transferred, and upon this the author of al-Durr proceeded, saying: The separate object is only washed, except a rough stone like a millstone, it is like earth.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement that defines the scope of 'earth' in the previous ruling. The word 'earth' customarily refers to objects embedded and fixed in the ground, such as embedded stones, bricks, and pebbles. If a stone or brick is separate and unattached, it is not considered earth and must be washed. However, there is a nuance: even separate stones that absorb impurity (like a rough millstone) are purified by drying, by analogy to earth. Non-absorbent stones like marble require washing. This detail is taken from al-Khaniyah and followed by later authorities.

Key Terms
الأرض (al-arḍ): Earth; includes the ground and materials embedded in it for the purpose of purification by drying.
الحصى (al-ḥaṣā): Pebbles; considered earth if embedded, but some scholars did not require embedding for them.
الجفاف (al-jafāf): Drying; a method of purification where the impurity's trace disappears through evaporation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the passage, which of the following statements is correct?
A) All separate stones require washing regardless of absorbency.
B) A separate absorbent stone like a millstone is purified by drying.
C) Pebbles are always considered earth even if not attached.
D) Marble tiles are purified by drying if they are fixed.

Key Takeaway
Purification by drying applies only to earth and its fixed components, with an exceptional extension to separate absorbent stones.

Purification by Istihalah: When Transformation of Essence Cleanses
Critical refinement on transformation of najasah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purification by istihalah, identify the intra-madhhab difference between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and apply the criterion of essence change to distinguish pure from impure transformed substances.

Core Idea
When a najis substance completely transforms into a new essence, it becomes pure—the ruling follows the essence.

This passage addresses a critical fiqh question: does a najasah become pure if its very substance changes into something else? For example, if impure wood burns to ash, or if impure salt forms from brine. The Hanafi school, particularly Imam Muhammad, says yes—by analogy to wine becoming vinegar. But there is an internal difference with Abu Yusuf, and an important exception for distilled liquids. Let's unpack the rule and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The text rules that a najasah becomes pure when its entire substance transforms into a different substance—examples: salt, earth, nitre, ash. This is the general principle of istihalah.
The ruling is for the transformed substance itself; the process is the transformation.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is qiyas on the well-known case of grape juice: it becomes wine (najis) and then vinegar (pure). The common illa is complete change of essence (tabaddul al-haqiqah).
This is an example of qiyas jaliy because the illa is evident.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad holds that transformation purifies. Abu Yusuf disagrees, maintaining that the original impurity persists. The text declares Muhammad's view as as-sahih (the sound) and mufta bihi.
The ikhtilaf is legitimate; both imams based their views on valid reasoning.
4
Apply the rule to special cases
Vapor from impure places (latrine, stable, bath) that condenses and drips is not najis by istihsan—because the droplets are not directly from the najasah but from the air. But intentionally distilled najis liquid (like 'araqi) remains najis because the essence did not fully change (it is concentrated impurity).
Distillation does not change the essence; it separates the impure components.
5
Derive the operative criterion
The key is complete transformation of the original substance. Mere dilution, evaporation, or separation does not suffice. The essence must become a new, different substance.
Use this criterion to evaluate any modern scenario involving chemical or physical changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتطهر نجاسة إستحالت عينها كأن صارت ملحاً أو تراباً أو طروناً أو احترقت بالنار فتصير رماداً طاهراً على الصحيح لتبدل الحقيقة كالعصير يصير خمراً فينجس ثم خلاً فيظهر وبخار الكنيف والإصطبل والحمام إذا قطر لا يكون نجساً إستحساناً والمستقطر من النجاسة نجس كالمسمى بالعرقي حرام
And a najasah whose substance has transformed is purified, such as if it becomes salt, or earth, or nitre, or is burned by fire, becoming ash – it is pure according to the sound view, due to the change of essence, like juice that becomes wine and becomes najis, then becomes vinegar and becomes pure. And the vapor of a latrine, stable, and bathhouse, if it drips, is not najis by istihsan. And what is distilled from najasah is najis, like what is called 'araqi, which is forbidden.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement on the ruling that a najis substance is purified if its entire essence changes into a different substance. Examples include salt, earth, or ash. The proof is by analogy to juice becoming wine (najis) and then vinegar (pure). This is the position of Imam Muhammad and is the fatwa. Abu Yusuf disagreed, holding that transformation does not purify. The text also discusses vapor from impure places: by istihsan, if it condenses and drips, it is not najis, but intentionally distilled najis liquid (like 'araqi) is najis. The rule is that if the essence changes, the impurity ruling follows the essence.

Key Terms
استحالة (istihālah): Transformation of the entire substance of a najasah into a different substance, leading to purification.
العرقي (al-ʿaraqī): A distilled alcoholic beverage made from dates or molasses; considered najis and haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, what is the ruling on najasah that has turned into ash after being burned?
A) It remains najis because burning does not remove impurity.
B) It becomes pure because the essence changed.
C) It is only pure if washed with water.
D) It is pure if burned completely, but not if partially.

Key Takeaway
Najasah becomes pure when its entire substance transforms into a different essence, as confirmed by the soundest Hanafi position.

Purification of Dry Mani (Semen) by Rubbing
Critical refinement on purification of dry mani by rubbing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purifying dry mani and apply the condition that differentiates between dry and wet, and between pure mani and mixed impurity.

Core Idea
Dry mani is purified by rubbing; wet mani must be washed — a concession based on the hadith.

This lesson examines a specific and practical ruling from the Hanafi school: the purification of dry semen (mani) from clothing and body. The school grants a concession (rukhsah) that dry mani can be removed by simply rubbing it off, based on a direct prophetic instruction. Understanding this ruling requires attention to conditions, such as the impurity being dry and not mixed with other sources of contamination.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is the purification of dry mani. The ruling is that rubbing (fark) suffices to make it pure.
Rubbing is only for dry mani; wet mani requires washing.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith: 'Wash it when wet, rub it when dry.' This is a direct command from the Prophet.
The hadith is ahad and zanni al-thubut, but accepted by Hanafis.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit command to rub when dry indicates that rubbing is sufficient for purification. The imperative form (ifraku) implies obligation but is understood as a concession.
Hanafi usul: al-amr yufidu al-wujub; here it is a recommended method.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is an intra-Hanafi disagreement: some scholars (like al-Fadli) held that a woman's mani is not purified by rubbing due to its thinness. Also, other schools require washing for all mani, considering it pure or requiring washing.
The sound view (sahih) and apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) support rubbing for both genders.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text states that the sound view (as-sahih) and the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) affirm rubbing for both male and female. This is the mufta bihi position.
Authority weight: zahir al-riwaya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويطهر المني الجاف ولو مني امرأة على الصحيح بفركه عن الثوب ولو جديداً مبطناً وعن البدن بفركه في ظاهر الرواية إن لم يتنجس بملطخ خارج المخرج كبول ويطهر المني الرطب بغسله لقوله : اغسليه رطباً وافركيه يابساً
And dry mani (semen) is purified, even if it is a woman's mani according to the sound view, by rubbing it from the garment, even if the garment is new and lined, and from the body by rubbing in the apparent narration, provided it is not contaminated with an external najasah like urine. And wet mani is purified by washing it, due to his saying: 'Wash it when wet, and rub it when dry.'
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement on the purification of mani (semen). Dry mani is purified by rubbing (fark) from both clothes and body, according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya). This is a special concession (rukhsah) because of the difficulty of washing, and is based on the hadith: 'Wash it when wet, and rub it when dry.' The ruling applies even to a woman's mani, though some restricted it to men. The garment may be new and lined; the place (like a mat) is analogous. However, if the mani is mixed with another impurity like urine, rubbing does not purify. Wet mani must be washed. The concession is only for dry mani.

Key Terms
الفرك (al-fark): Rubbing; a method of removing dry impurity, especially mani, by hand until it crumbles.
المني (al-manī): Semen; considered najis in the Hanafi school, but with a special concession for dry removal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It must be washed with water three times.
B) It is purified by rubbing it dry.
C) It becomes pure only after the garment is washed in a specific manner.
D) It cannot be purified and the garment must be discarded.

Key Takeaway
Dry mani is purified by rubbing; the concession is based on a direct hadith and applies even to women's mani according to the sound view.
