Purification of Impurity by Drying on Earth: Condition of Complete Dryness
Refining the condition for purification by drying

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for purification of najasah by drying on earth (jafaf) and identify which surfaces this ruling applies to.

Core Idea
Impurity on earth becomes pure only when completely dry—mere surface dryness is not enough.

The Hanafi school holds that under certain conditions, najasah (impurity) can become purified simply by drying on the ground, without washing. However, this is not absolute; there are specific requirements. This lesson examines the relied-upon view from the commentary of al-Tahtawi, refining the understanding of what 'drying' entails and which surfaces qualify.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the ruling on najasah when it dries on earth? State the Hanafi position that the impurity becomes pure by drying.
The base text states this as the 'mukhtar' (chosen view).
2
Determine the condition for purification
What exactly is required for purification? The passage clarifies that 'jafaf' means complete disappearance of dampness, not just surface dryness. This is the condition (shart).
Al-Qahistani says 'the better expression is jafaf (disappearance of dampness)'.
3
Understand the refinement
Why is mere 'dryness' not enough? The apparent meaning of the base text might be misinterpreted. The refinement ensures that the impurity is fully dry, with no residual dampness.
The impurity's moisture, not the tree's moisture, is the subject.
4
Apply the scope
Which surfaces are included? The ruling applies to earth and anything fixed in it: walls, roof enclosures (khuss), and even attached doors, as long as they are stationary.
The commentator extends by analogy to doors.
5
Note the alternative view
There is a differing opinion that washing is necessary (qīla lā budda min ghaslihi). This is the weaker view; the relied-upon view is drying.
This alternative may come from other madhhabs or a minority Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النجاسة لأيبسه عن رطوبته وذهاب أثرها تبعاً للأرض على المختار، وقيل: لا بد من غسله. قال الأخير على ما كان عليه من زواله فلا يجوز التيمم بها قوله : (لا يبسه عن رطوبته) ظاهره أنه يكفي فيها الجفاف مع بقاء النداوة وليس كذلك. قال القهستاني والأحسن التعبير بالجفاف أي ذهاب النداوة فإنه المشروط إلا أن يقال مراده أنه لا يشترط جفاف رطوبة الشجر، بل جفاف رطوبة النجاسة. قوله : ( وذهاب أثرها عطف على قوله بجفافه. قوله : (تبعاً للأرض) يلحق بما ذكر في هذا الحكم كل ما كان ثابتاً فيها كالحيطان والخص بالخاء المعجمة وهو حجيزة السطح وغير ذلك ما . دام قائماً عليها فيظهر بالجفاف وذهاب الأثر هو المختار اهـ قلت وهذا يقتضي أن نحو الأبواب المتصلة كذلك كذا بحثه بعض الأفاضل
The impurity, by its drying from its moisture and disappearance of its trace, following the earth, on the chosen view. And it was said: It is necessary to wash it. The other said: Upon what it was of its removal, so tayammum with it is not permissible. His statement: (its drying from its moisture) apparently it suffices dryness with remaining dampness, but that is not the case. Al-Qahistani said: The better expression is 'drying' i.e., disappearance of dampness, for that is the condition. Unless it is said that he means he does not require drying of the tree's moisture, rather drying of the impurity's moisture. His statement: (and disappearance of its trace) is conjuncted to his statement 'by its drying'. His statement: (following the earth) includes in this ruling everything that is fixed in it, like walls and the khuss (with the dotted kha') which is the enclosure of the roof and other things, as long as it remains upon it, so it becomes pure by drying and disappearance of trace is the chosen view. I say: This implies that similar things like attached doors are likewise, as some virtuous ones investigated.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage from al-Tahtawi's hashia on Maraqi al-Falah deals with the purification of najasah by drying on earth. The base text states that najasah, when it dries on the ground and its trace disappears, becomes pure on the relied-upon view (al-mukhtar). There is an opposing view that requires washing. The commentator then explains the nuance of drying: it requires the disappearance of dampness, not just surface dryness. He also clarifies that the ruling applies not only to earth but also to any fixed structure like walls and roofs, and by extension, attached doors, as long as the impurity dries and leaves no trace. This is a Hanafi ruling; other schools may differ on the sufficiency of drying.

Key Terms
النجاسة (al-najasah): Impurity that prevents validity of prayer unless removed.
الجفاف (al-jafaf): Complete dryness with disappearance of dampness; the condition for purification by drying on earth.
النداوة (al-nadawah): Dampness; the residual moisture that must disappear for purification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only the disappearance of the impurity's trace
B) The impurity must be washed with water
C) Complete dryness without any residual dampness
D) The impurity must be exposed to direct sunlight

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows purification of najasah by complete drying on fixed surfaces, provided both dampness and trace disappear.

Purification by Transformation (Istihala) of Najasah
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on istihala purifying najasah, explain the dispute between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and apply the principle to new cases like salt from impure water or distilled liquids.

Core Idea
Complete transformation of an impurity's essence removes its impurity — this is Muhammad's view, chosen for fatwa.

Istihala refers to a complete change in the substance of an impurity, such as water evaporating and leaving salt, or bones burning to ash. The Hanafi school, following Imam Muhammad, rules that such transformation purifies. Abu Yusuf disagreed, and the commentary clarifies the proof and practical applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The matn states: najasah is purified when its very substance transforms (like becoming salt, earth, or ash). Determine the hukm wad'i — the transformed substance is tahir.
This is not a taklifi ruling (fard/wajib) but a declaratory ruling of purity.
2
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Muhammad holds istihala purifies; Abu Yusuf does not. Identify each position and note that Muhammad's is the mufta bihi.
Al-Tahtawi explicitly says 'wa huwa al-mukhtar lil-fatwa'.
3
Examine the proof (qiyas)
The proof is qiyas to the agreed-upon case of juice becoming wine (impure) then vinegar (pure). The illah is 'change of reality' (tabaddul al-haqiqah).
This is qiyas jaliy: the asl is juice→vinegar, the far' is any impurity→new substance.
4
Apply the principle to exceptions
Note that distillation (mustaqtar) does not purify — the substance does not change completely. Likewise, vapor from impure places is ruled pure by istihsan, not by istihala.
The egg of a non-edible bird is disputed: one view says impure (like meat), another pure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتطهر نجاسة استحالت عينها كأن صارت (ملحاً أو تراباً أو طروناً (أو احترقت بالنار) فتصير رماداً طاهراً على الصحيح لتبدل الحقيقة كالعصير يصير خمراً فينجس، ثم خلاً فيظهر وبخار الكنيف والإصطبل والحمام إذا قطر لا يكون نجساً إستحساناً والمستقطر من النجاسة نجس كالمسمى بالعرقي حرام وبيض ما لا يؤكل قيل نجس كلحمه، وقيل طاهر. قوله: (وتظهر نجاسة استحالت عينها) فيجوز الانتفاع بها، وهذا قول محمد وهو المختار للفتوى لأن زوال الحقيقة يستتبع زوال الوصف، وقال أبو يوسف : لا تظهر . قوله: (كالعصير هذا استدلال بثبوت النظير المتفق عليه قوله : (كالمسمى بالعرقي) ويحد شاربه إذا سكر منه وهو نجس نجاسة مغلظة على ما ذكره العلامة الإسقاطي في كتاب الحظر من حاشيته على مثلا مسكين
And impurity whose substance transforms is purified, such as if it becomes salt, earth, or natron, or is burned by fire and becomes ash – it is pure on the correct view due to change of reality, like juice becomes wine and is impure, then vinegar and becomes pure. And the vapor of the latrine, stable, and bathhouse, if it drips, is not impure by istihsan. And what is distilled from impurity is impure, like what is called 'al-araqi' (arrack), which is forbidden. And eggs of what is not eaten: it was said they are impure like its meat, and it was said they are pure. His statement: (And impurity transforms) so it is permissible to benefit from it, and this is the view of Muhammad and it is the chosen view for fatwa because the disappearance of reality entails the disappearance of the description. And Abu Yusuf said: it does not become pure. His statement: (like juice) this is a proof by establishing the agreed-upon parallel. His statement: (like what is called al-araqi) and its drinker is punished with hadd if he becomes intoxicated from it, and it is heavy impurity (najis maghlazah) as mentioned by al-Isqati in Kitab al-Hazr from his hashia on Matalib al-Masakin.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage from al-Tahtawi's hashia addresses the concept of istihala (transformation). The base matn states that if the very substance of an impurity changes into something else (e.g., salt, earth, ash), it becomes pure because its reality has changed. The commentator notes that this is the view of Imam Muhammad, which is the chosen position for fatwa, while Abu Yusuf holds that transformation does not purify. The proof of this principle is the agreed-upon analogy of grape juice becoming wine (impure) then turning into vinegar (pure). The commentator also mentions derivative rulings: steam from impure places is not impure by istihsan, but distilled liquor remains impure. Additionally, eggs of non-edible birds: one view says they are impure like meat, another says pure.

Key Terms
استحالة (istihala): Complete transformation of the substance of an impurity into something else, resulting in purification.
التبدل الحقيقة (tabaddul al-haqiqah): Change of the essential nature of a substance, which removes the ruling of impurity.
المستقطر (al-mustaqtar): Liquid distilled from an impure source; it remains impure in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, which of the following would be considered purified by istihala?
A) Water obtained by distilling impure water
B) Ash from burning a dead animal
C) Steam condensed from a latrine
D) Arrack (distilled liquor) from grapes

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, complete transformation of an impurity's essence (istihala) renders the new substance pure, based on the analogy of wine turning to vinegar, while mere physical changes like distillation do not suffice.

The Impurity of Semen and Its Purification by Rubbing
Inter-madhhab dispute on semen purity with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the impurity of semen, state the evidence from hadith, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i/Maliki view.

Core Idea
Semen is najis and requires removal, but can be purified by rubbing when dry—based on Aisha's practice.

This passage addresses a well-known inter-madhhab disagreement regarding the impurity of semen. The Hanafi school, relying on the hadith of Aisha (RA), holds that semen is najis and must be removed from the garment before prayer. The Shafi'i and Maliki schools consider it pure. The commentary also provides important details about the conditions for purification by rubbing (farak) and responds to the objection that semen is the origin of humans.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on semen
Identify the Hanafi ruling: semen is impure (najis) and must be removed from the garment. Purification can be done by rubbing when dry or washing when wet.
This is the mufta bihi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Identify the hadith evidence
Find the hadith of Aisha (RA) in the passage: she washed wet semen and scratched dry semen from the Prophet's garment. Explain how this proves impurity (the act of washing indicates removal of impurity).
The hadith is in Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Identify the opposing school(s) and their position
State that Malik, Ahmad (in one narration), and al-Shafi'i consider semen pure and not requiring washing.
Their argument may be based on semen being the origin of human creation.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the objection
The Hanafi response: human honor comes after development from an impure fluid; the Quran calls it 'despicable fluid' (māʾin mahīn). Impurity does not diminish honor.
This refutes the objection that something originating from humans cannot be impure.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice, Hanafis require removing semen from the garment for prayer validity; other schools do not require it, making prayer valid even with semen present.
This is a real-world impact on worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصابه الماء بعد الفرك فهو ونظائره كالأرض إذا جفت وجلد الميتة المشمس، والبئر إذا غارت وقد اختلف التصحيح والأولى إعتبار الطهارة في الكل كما تفيده المتون وملاقاة الطاهر طاهراً مثله لا توجب التنجيس. بالفرك لعدم الضرورة وقيد بقوله بملطخ الخ لأنه لو بال ولم ينتشر البول على رأس الذكر بأن لم يتجاوز الثقب أو انتشر لكن خرج المني دفقاً من غير أن ينتشر على رأس الذكر، فإنه يطهر بالفرك لأنه لم يوجد سوى مروره على البول في مجراه ولا أثر لذلك في الباطن كما في التبيين، والبحر وحكي الشرح والسيد ذلك بقيل فقالا : وقيل لو بال، ولم ينتشر بوله على رأس الذكر الخ. قوله : لقوله لا إلخ ) قال الكمال : الله أعلم بصحته ومراده بهذا اللفظ وإلا فالمدعي ثابت بمعناه فقد ورد في الصحيحين عن عائشة رضي الله عنها أنها كانت تغسل المني من ثوب رسول الله ، ولمسلم من وجه آخر عنها لقد رأيتني واني لأحكه من ثوب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يابساً بظفري، وروى البزار والدارقطني عنها أيضاً قالت كنت أفرك المني من ثوب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا كان يابساً وأغسله إذا كان رطباً وبقولنا : قال مالك وأحمد في إحدى الروايتين، وقال الشافعي وأحمد في رواية : هو طاهر لا يجب غسله، ولا يشكل على قولنا بنجاسته أنه أصل خلقة الإنسان لأن تكريمه يحصل بعد تطوره الأطوار المعلومة من المائية والعلقية، والمضغية ولأن تخليقه في الأصل من شيء نجس، ثم تشريفه بأنواع الكرامات أبلغ في المنة، وإليه الإشارة بقوله تعالى : ألم نخلقكم من ماء مهين) [المرسلات : ٧٧] على | لو قلنا ان النجس ما لم يتخلف منه الإنسان لم يضرنا ونتخلص من قبح التلفظ بأن أصل خلقة الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام نجس كما في الحلبي .
If water touches it after rubbing, then it and its similar cases are pure, like earth when it dries, the sun-dried skin of a dead animal, and the well when its water disappears - and verification has differed, and the preferred view is to consider purity in all as indicated by the matn texts, and a pure thing meeting another pure thing does not cause impurity. By rubbing for lack of necessity, and it is restricted by his statement 'with something smeared' etc. because if he urinates and the urine does not spread over the head of the penis, either not exceeding the opening, or it spreads but semen comes out in a gush without spreading over the head of the penis, then it is purified by rubbing because there is only its passing over the urine in its passage and that has no effect in the interior as in al-Tabyin and al-Bahr; and al-Sharh and al-Sayyid narrated that with 'qila' and they said: it is said if he urinated and his urine did not spread over the head of the penis etc. His statement: 'because his statement [does not] etc.' (al-Kamal said: Allah knows best about its authenticity, and what he meant by this wording; otherwise the claim is established by its meaning, for it is reported in the two Sahihs from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that she used to wash semen from the garment of the Messenger of Allah, and in Muslim via another chain from her: 'I saw myself scratching dried semen from the garment of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) with my fingernail.' And al-Bazzar and al-Daraqutni narrated from her also that she said: 'I used to rub semen from the garment of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) when it was dry, and wash it when it was wet.' And in our opinion: Malik and Ahmad in one of two narrations said, and al-Shafi'i and Ahmad in a narration said: it is pure and does not require washing. And it does not create a problem for our view of its impurity that it is the origin of human creation, because its honor comes after its development through the known stages of water, clot, and chewed lump; and because its creation originally from something impure, then its honoring with various kinds of honors is more emphatic in grace, and to this refers the saying of Allah Almighty: 'Did We not create you from a despicable fluid?' (al-Mursalat: 77). Even if we said that what is impure is that from which no human develops, it would not harm us, and we would escape the ugliness of saying that the origin of the creation of the prophets (peace be upon them) is impure, as in al-Halabi.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the impurity of semen (mani). The Hanafi position is that semen is impure (najis) and can be purified by rubbing (farak) when dry, or by washing when wet. The author begins by quoting the matn which lists various cases of purification by non-liquid means, including the case of semen being purified by water after rubbing. The commentary then focuses on the specific issue of semen. It clarifies that the ruling of purification by rubbing applies only when there is no necessity (like using a tissue) and when the urine has not spread over the head of the penis; otherwise washing is required. The hadith evidence from Aisha (RA) is presented: she washed semen from the Prophet's garment and also scratched it dry. This establishes that semen is impure. The passage then contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Malik, Ahmad, and al-Shafi'i, who consider semen pure and not requiring washing. The Hanafi response to the objection that semen is the origin of humans is that the honor comes after development stages, and the origin being from something impure does not detract from the final honored state, as indicated by Quran 77:20. The author also notes that if we define impure as something from which no human is formed, it would not change the ruling.

Key Terms
المني (al-maniyy): Semen, the fluid emitted during ejaculation. It is considered impure (najis) in the Hanafi school.
الفرك (al-fark): Rubbing, specifically the act of removing dried semen from a garment by scratching or rubbing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on semen (mani) that gets on a garment?
A) It is pure and does not require any action.
B) It is impure and must be washed or rubbed when dry.
C) It is only impure if it comes from animals.
D) It is permissible to pray with it if it is dry.

Key Takeaway
Semen is najis in the Hanafi school and must be removed from the garment; purification is achieved by rubbing when dry or washing when wet, supported by the practice of Aisha (RA).

Mutahhirat: Multiple Means of Purification and the Principle of Pure Meeting Pure
Lists purifiers and refines a grammatical point

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list at least five purifiers in Hanafi fiqh beyond water and explain the grammatical difference in the idafa construction of 'ملاقاة الطاهر'.

Core Idea
Water is not the only purifier; Hanafi fiqh recognizes over thirty means, each with specific conditions.

While the Qur'an and Sunnah primarily mention water for purification, the Hanafi school expands the list based on the principle of removing impurity by any effective means. This passage enumerates these purifiers in a poetic form and clarifies a grammatical point about the phrase 'ملاقاة الطاهر' (the meeting of the pure). By studying this, you will appreciate the breadth of Hanafi rulings on purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main topics
The passage covers two related topics: the enumeration of mutahhirat (purifiers) beyond water, and the grammatical analysis of the phrase 'ملاقاة الطاهر' that states a principle: a pure object meeting another pure object does not transmit impurity.
The poetic list is a mnemonic for students.
2
Parse the poetic list of purifiers
From the verse, extract each method: washing, wiping, drying, scraping, turning the eye (flipping), digging, tanning, pickling, slaughter, rubbing, massaging, entering, transformation, carding, drawing water, fire, boiling, washing part. Understand their specific applications (e.g., tanning for skins, drying for surfaces).
Some terms are obscure; context from Hanafi fiqh clarifies them.
3
Understand the principle of pure meeting pure
The phrase 'ملاقاة الطاهر' is analyzed grammatically: the word 'طاهر' can be in the nominative (subject) or accusative (object) case, affecting whether the idafa is subjective ('the pure meeting' i.e., pure object meeting another) or objective ('meeting the pure'). The ruling: a pure object meeting another pure object does not become impure.
The grammatical nuance shows precision in Hanafi legal language.
4
Apply the principle
If you have a dry, pure cloth and it touches dry, pure earth, the cloth remains pure. The principle reinforces that impurity only transfers under specific conditions (e.g., wetness).
This principle is a building block for many taharah rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ونظائره) أي من كل ما حكم بطهارته بغير مائع كما في الدر. قال : وقد أنهيت المطهرات إلى نيف وثلاثين نظمتها فقلت : وغسل وسمح والجفاف مطهر ونحت وقلب العين والحفر يذكر ودبغ وتخليل ذكاة تخلل وفرك ودلك والدخول التغور تصرفه في البعض ندف ونزحها ونار وغلي غسل بعض تقوّر قوله : ( وملاقاة الطاهر) كالماء وقوله : طاهراً مثله كالأرض إذا جفت ونظائره وقوله : طاهر في بعض نسخ بالرفع فهو فاعل والإضافة من إضافة المصدر إلى مفعوله وفي نسخ بالنصب مفعول والإضافة من إضافة المصدر إلى فاعله .
His statement: 'and its similar cases' meaning everything that is ruled pure by means other than a liquid, as in al-Durr. He said: I have enumerated the purifiers up to thirty-something and versified them, saying: Washing, wiping, and drying are purifiers, and scraping, turning the eye, and digging are mentioned, and tanning, pickling, slaughter, pickling, rubbing, massaging, and entering the penetration, its transformation in some, carding, drawing water, fire, boiling, washing part, it is made. His statement: 'and the meeting of the pure' like water; and his statement: 'pure like it' like earth when it dries and its similar cases; and his statement: 'pure' in some copies is raised (nominative), so it is the subject, and the idafa is from adding the masdar to its object; and in some copies it is accusative, and the idafa is from adding the masdar to its subject.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a definition and enumeration of the various means of purification (mutahhirat) in Hanafi fiqh, besides water. The commentator explains that 'نظائره' refers to anything that can become pure without using a liquid. He then provides a poetic list of purifiers, which includes: washing (ghusl), wiping (simah), drying (jafaf), scraping (naht), turning the eye (qalb al-ayn) - meaning flipping the skin to remove impurity, digging (hafr) - for a well that becomes impure, tanning (dabgh) - for animal skins, pickling (takhlil) - for impure vinegar to become pure through transformation, slaughter (tadhkiyah) - for animals, rubbing (fark) - for dry semen, massaging (dalk) - for a garment that is smeared with impurity, entering (dukhul) - like entering a bathhouse? Actually 'dukhul al-taghawwur' is obscure, probably entering a cave? Carding (nadf) - for wool, drawing water (nazh) - from a well, fire (nar) - for things like bricks, boiling (ghaliy) - for cooking, washing part (ghusl ba'd) - washing a part of the garment? The exact meanings require reference. Then the commentator discusses the grammar of 'ملاقاة الطاهر', explaining that the word 'طاهر' can be in the nominative or accusative, affecting whether the idafa construction is subjective or objective. The core ruling is that when a pure object meets another pure object, it does not become impure.

Key Terms
المطهرات (al-mutahhirat): The various agents or actions that can make something pure after it has become impure. They include water and other methods.
الملاقاة (al-mulaqat): The meeting or contact between two objects. The principle stated is that a pure object meeting another pure object does not transfer impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Nominative (الرفع)
B) Accusative (النصب)
C) Genitive (الجر)
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh recognizes over thirty purifiers, and the principle 'ملاقاة الطاهر' emphasizes that a pure object meeting another pure object does not transfer impurity, with grammatical precision underscoring legal clarity.

Elephant and Dog Skins: The Hanafi Dispute on Tanning
Intra-Hanafi dispute on elephant and dog skin purification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi majority position on the purification of elephant and dog skins by tanning and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute with its practical consequence.

Core Idea
Elephant skin is purified by tanning; dog skin is not intrinsically impure—both can become pure.

In this lesson, we examine a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute over the status of the elephant's skin after tanning. While the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) considers the elephant like any other predator—pure after tanning—Imam Muhammad holds it to be najis al-ayn like the pig. We will explore the evidence and the practical difference in rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The issue is whether the skin of an elephant becomes pure by tanning or remains intrinsically impure. Also, the status of dog skin is discussed: is it najis al-ayn?
2
State the Hanafi majority position
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: Elephant is like other predators; its skin is purified by tanning, and its bone (ivory) is pure. Dog skin is also purified because the dog is not najis al-ayn according to the correct view.
The text says 'في الأصح' (the most correct view).
3
State the dissenting view
Imam Muhammad: Elephant is najis al-ayn like the pig because it is forbidden and not generally benefitted from.
His reasoning is analogy (qiyas) with the pig.
4
Analyse the evidence for the majority
The proof is the hadith about people combing with ivory combs, showing that the bone of the elephant is pure. If it were impure like the pig, it would not be used for combing. Also the principle that the exterior of every animal is pure until death.
Al-Fath states this hadith invalidates Muhammad's view.
5
Identify the practical consequence
If one prays with a small puppy in his sleeve, the prayer is valid according to the majority (since puppy skin is pure after tanning) but invalid according to Muhammad's view (if considered impure). Al-Hindawani stipulates the puppy's mouth must be closed.
This shows the thamarah al-khilaf (fruit of the dispute).

His statement: 'because it is like other predators' – that is, this indicates the purity of its bone, for if it were like the pig, it would not be combed with its bone. He said in al-Fath: This hadith invalidates Muhammad's view of the elephant's intrinsic impurity. His statement: 'of ivory' – al-Muhkam says: it is the tusks of the elephant, and nothing other than the tusk is called ivory. Al-Jawhari says: it is the bone of the elephant, one piece is called ‘aja. That is what the author followed. And ivory is also applied to the shell, the back of the sea tortoise, as al-Asma’i said, and al-Sahib al-Misbah transmitted it. The Shafi’is apply to it what is reported that Fatima (may Allah be pleased with her) had a bracelet of ivory. His statement: 'because it is not najis al-ayn according to the correct view.' Al-Nahr from al-Muhit: and some attribute that to the Imam, and the view of impurity is to them (the two). And the effect of the difference appears in the case where one prays with a small puppy in his sleeve: it is valid according to the first view, not the second. Al-Hindawani stipulated that it be closed-mouthed. His statement: 'by tanning' – with kasra, it and al-dabagh and al-dabgh (with kasr) is what is used for tanning, and al-dabagha is also the craft. His statement: 'like al-qaraz' – with the pointed za, and whoever pronounces it with dad is mistaken. One piece is qarazah: a known grain that comes out in a pod like lentils from the 'idah tree. His statement: 'and it is the leaves of al-salam' – there is laxity in this, because leaves are called khabat among them, and it is used for fodder, not for tanning. His statement: 'and al-shabb' – with a single ba, and it is a mineral that Allah has grown in the earth resembling alum. Al-Azhari said it, and al-shath with the triple-letter tha is a plant with a pleasant smell and bitter taste used for tanning, as al-Jawhari said. Among real tanners is salt and similar things that remove stench and moisture, as in al-Qahistani. He added in al-Siraj: and it prevents the return of decay to the skin when water enters it. Al-Tabyin says: if it dries and does not transform (i.e., its stench does not go away, as al-Shalabi interpreted), it does not become pure. And there is no difference in the tanner between Muslim, disbeliever, child, insane person, and woman, if the purpose of tanning is achieved. If a disbeliever tans it and it is thought likely that he tanned it with something impure, then it is washed. And the absorbent cloth, as in al-Khulasa. And in Munyat al-Musalli and its commentary: if a squirrel comes out from dar al-harb and it is known that it was tanned with the fat of a dead animal, prayer is not valid in it until...
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the purification of the skin of a dead elephant by tanning. The majority view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is that the elephant is like other predators: its skin is purified by tanning, and its bone (ivory) is pure. Imam Muhammad holds that the elephant is intrinsically impure (najis al-ayn) like the pig because it is unlawful and not used for benefit. The proof for the majority is the practice of using ivory combs, indicating that the bone is pure. The text then states that the correct view and fatwa are that the elephant's skin is purified by tanning. It also discusses the dog: its skin is not intrinsically impure and can be purified by tanning (even though the dog is not eaten). The passage then details the requirements for tanning: real tanning with astringent materials like qaraz (acacia pods), alum, etc., and legal tanning by drying in the sun or exposure to air. The effect of the dispute is shown in the example of praying with a small puppy in the sleeve: if the puppy is considered pure (by the first view), the prayer is valid; if impure (by the second), it is not. Conditions are stipulated by al-Hindawani that the puppy's mouth be closed. Additionally, the tanner's religion does not matter; if a non-Muslim tans with impure substances, the skin must be washed. If the skin is known to be tanned with the fat of a dead animal, prayer is not valid.

Key Terms
الدباغة (al-dabaghah): The process of tanning, which purifies the skin of a dead animal by removing impurities and making it fit for use.
العاج (al-'aj): Ivory, specifically the tusks of the elephant, which are considered pure according to the majority Hanafi view.
نجس العين (najis al-'ayn): Intrinsically impure; an object that is impure in itself and cannot be purified by any means (like the pig and dog in the Hanafi school).

Key Takeaway
The skin of an elephant is purified by tanning according to the majority Hanafi view, and ivory is pure.

The Process of Tanning: Conditions and Materials
Detailed rules of tanning process and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for effective tanning, distinguish between real and legal tanning, and apply the ruling on the tanner's identity and washing requirements.

Core Idea
Tanning purifies skin through real (astringent) or legal (sun/air) methods; the tanner's religion does not affect purity, but suspected impure materials require washing.

In this lesson, we delve into the practical rules of tanning as outlined in the Hanafi school. We learn that tanning can be real—using astringent substances that remove stench and moisture—or legal, such as exposure to sun and air. The tanner can be anyone, but if there is reason to suspect impurity, the skin must be washed.

Guided Path
1
Define real tanning
Real tanning uses substances like qaraz (acacia pods), alum, salt, oak galls, pomegranate peels, etc., that remove stench and moisture and prevent decay.
The text also mentions 'shath' (a plant) and 'shabb' (alum).
2
Define legal tanning
Legal tanning includes dusting (tatrib), sunning (tashmis), and leaving in the air (ilqa' fi al-hawa'). These methods also achieve purification.
The text says 'بالدباغة الحكمية'.
3
Identify the condition for purity
The skin must lose its stench and decay. If it dries but still stinks, it is not pure. Al-Tabyin states: 'لو جف، ولم يستحل... لم يطهر'.
The condition is the removal of stench and moisture.
4
Examine the tanner's identity
There is no difference in the tanner between Muslim, disbeliever, child, insane person, or woman, as long as the tanning purpose is achieved.
The text says 'لا فرق في الدايخ بين مسلم، وكافر وصبي، ومجنون وامرأة'.
5
Apply the washing rule
If a disbeliever tans and it is thought likely that he used something impure, the skin must be washed. If the skin is known to be tanned with the fat of a dead animal, prayer on it is invalid.
The example of a squirrel from dar al-harb is given.

Source Text

(بالدباغة) بالكسر، هي والدباغ والدبغ بالكسر ما يدبغ به والدباغة أيضاً الصناعة قوله : (كالقرظ) بالظاء المشالة، وصحف من نطق بها ضاداً الواحدة قرظة حب معروف يخرج في غلاف كالعدس من شجر العضاه قوله: (وهو ورق السلم) فيه تسامح فإن الورق يسمى الخبط عندهم وهو يعلف به ولا يدبغ به قوله : (والشب) بالباء الوحدة وهو من الجواهر التي أنبتها الله تعالى في الأرض يشبه الزاج. قاله الأزهري والشث بالثاء المثلثة نبت طيب الرائحة مر الطعم يدبغ به قاله الجوهري ومن الدابغ الحقيقي الملح وشبهه من كل ما يزيل النتن والرطوبة كما في القهستاني، زاد في السراج، ويمنع عود الفساد إلى الجلد عند حصول الماء فيه قال في التبيين: لو جف، ولم يستحل أي لم يزل نتنه كما فسره الشلبي لم يطهر، ولا فرق في الدايخ بين مسلم، وكافر وصبي، ومجنون وامرأة إذا حصل المقصود من الدباغ فإن دبغه كافر، وغلب على ظنه أنه دبغه بشيء نجس فإنه يغسل، والتشرب عفوكما في الخلاصة، وفي منية المصلي، وشرحها السنجاب إذا خرج من دار الحرب وعلم أنه مدبوغ بودك الميتة لا تجوز به الصلاة ما لم
The skin of a dead animal, even if it is an elephant, because it is like other predators according to the most correct view, because were it [like the pig] it would not be combed with a comb of ivory, which is the bone of the elephant. And the skin of the dog is purified because it is not najis al-ayn (intrinsically impure) according to the correct view. By real tanning (like qaraz) which is the leaves of the salam tree or the fruit of the sant tree, and oak galls, pomegranate peels, and alum. And by legal tanning like dusting, sunning, and leaving in the air, so prayer is permissible in it and on it, and wudu from it. The skin of a dead animal. His statement: 'even if it is an elephant' – this is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa), and Muhammad said it is najis al-ayn like the pig because it is forbidden and not benefitted from. His statement: 'because it is like other predators' – that is, this indicates the purity of its bone, for if it were like the pig, it would not be combed with its bone. He said in al-Fath: This hadith invalidates Muhammad's view of the elephant's intrinsic impurity. His statement: 'of ivory' – al-Muhkam says: it is the tusks of the elephant, and nothing other than the tusk is called ivory. Al-Jawhari says: it is the bone of the elephant, one piece is called ‘aja. That is what the author followed. And ivory is also applied to the shell, the back of the sea tortoise, as al-Asma’i said, and al-Sahib al-Misbah transmitted it. The Shafi’is apply to it what is reported that Fatima (may Allah be pleased with her) had a bracelet of ivory. His statement: 'because it is not najis al-ayn according to the correct view.' And the fatwa is upon it, as in al-Bahr from al-Wahbaniyya, because the exterior of every animal is pure and does not become impure except by death, and its interior impurity in its stomach does not become apparent, like the impurity of the interior of a prayer mat. Al-Nahr from al-Muhit: and some attribute that to the Imam, and the view of impurity is to them (the two). And the effect of the difference appears in the case where one prays with a small puppy in his sleeve: it is valid according to the first view, not the second. Al-Hindawani stipulated that it be closed-mouthed. His statement: 'by tanning' – with kasra, it and al-dabagh and al-dabgh (with kasr) is what is used for tanning, and al-dabagha is also the craft. His statement: 'like al-qaraz' – with the pointed za, and whoever pronounces it with dad is mistaken. One piece is qarazah: a known grain that comes out in a pod like lentils from the 'idah tree. His statement: 'and it is the leaves of al-salam' – there is laxity in this, because leaves are called khabat among them, and it is used for fodder, not for tanning. His statement: 'and al-shabb' – with a single ba, and it is a mineral that Allah has grown in the earth resembling alum. Al-Azhari said it, and al-shath with the triple-letter tha is a plant with a pleasant smell and bitter taste used for tanning, as al-Jawhari said. Among real tanners is salt and similar things that remove stench and moisture, as in al-Qahistani. He added in al-Siraj: and it prevents the return of decay to the skin when water enters it. Al-Tabyin says: if it dries and does not transform (i.e., its stench does not go away, as al-Shalabi interpreted), it does not become pure. And there is no difference in the tanner between Muslim, disbeliever, child, insane person, and woman, if the purpose of tanning is achieved. If a disbeliever tans it and it is thought likely that he tanned it with something impure, then it is washed. And the absorbent cloth, as in al-Khulasa. And in Munyat al-Musalli and its commentary: if a squirrel comes out from dar al-harb and it is known that it was tanned with the fat of a dead animal, prayer is not valid in it until...
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the purification of the skin of a dead elephant by tanning. The majority view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is that the elephant is like other predators: its skin is purified by tanning, and its bone (ivory) is pure. Imam Muhammad holds that the elephant is intrinsically impure (najis al-ayn) like the pig because it is unlawful and not used for benefit. The proof for the majority is the practice of using ivory combs, indicating that the bone is pure. The text then states that the correct view and fatwa are that the elephant's skin is purified by tanning. It also discusses the dog: its skin is not intrinsically impure and can be purified by tanning (even though the dog is not eaten). The passage then details the requirements for tanning: real tanning with astringent materials like qaraz (acacia pods), alum, etc., and legal tanning by drying in the sun or exposure to air. The effect of the dispute is shown in the example of praying with a small puppy in the sleeve: if the puppy is considered pure (by the first view), the prayer is valid; if impure (by the second), it is not. Conditions are stipulated by al-Hindawani that the puppy's mouth be closed. Additionally, the tanner's religion does not matter; if a non-Muslim tans with impure substances, the skin must be washed. If the skin is known to be tanned with the fat of a dead animal, prayer is not valid.

Key Terms
الدباغة (al-dabaghah): The process of tanning, which purifies the skin of a dead animal by removing impurities and making it fit for use.
العاج (al-'aj): Ivory, specifically the tusks of the elephant, which are considered pure according to the majority Hanafi view.
نجس العين (najis al-'ayn): Intrinsically impure; an object that is impure in itself and cannot be purified by any means (like the pig and dog in the Hanafi school).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a non-Muslim tans a skin and it is thought he used impure materials, what must be done?
A) The skin is automatically pure.
B) The skin must be washed.
C) The skin is invalid and cannot be purified.
D) The skin must be tanned again by a Muslim.

Key Takeaway
Tanning is effective only if it removes stench and moisture; the tanner's identity is irrelevant, but suspected impurity requires washing.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Tanning Purifies Dead Animal Skins: General Ruling and Proof
Ruling on purification of dead animal skins by tanning with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that tanning purifies the skin of a dead animal, cite the hadith proof, and identify the conditions for effective tanning.

Core Idea
Tanning transforms a dead animal's skin from impure to pure, making it permissible for prayer and use.

Imagine you find a naturally dead sheep and have its skin tanned. Can you pray on it? The Hanafi school answers yes, based on a clear hadith. This lesson unpacks the general principle that tanning removes impurity (najasah) from all animal skins except pig and human, and explains the types of tanning that are effective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is tanning (dibagh) of the skin of a dead animal. According to the Hanafi school, after proper tanning, the skin becomes pure (tahir) and permissible to use in prayer.
This is the general ruling; exceptions (pig, human) will be covered in another lesson.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith: 'Any hide that is tanned has become pure' (أيما إهاب دبغ فقد طهر). This is a general statement (amm) covering all hides.
The hadith is recorded by al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, and others from Ibn Abbas.
3
Understand the conditions for valid tanning
Tanning can be actual (using substances like qaraz, alum, salt that remove moisture and stench) or legal (sunning, dusting, exposure to air). Both are effective if they achieve the purpose of purification.
The tanner can be Muslim or non-Muslim; the key is that the process achieves the intended result.
4
Note the effect of tanning
After valid tanning, prayer is permissible in (wearing) the skin and on it (as a prayer mat), and ablution from it (if used as a water skin) is valid. The purification applies both outwardly and inwardly, unlike the Maliki view which only purifies the outward.
This is a practical difference: Hanafis can use tanned hides for prayer mats; Malikis only pray on them (not in them).
5
Recognize the scope of the ruling
The ruling applies to the skin of any dead animal that is not inherently impure (najis al-ayn). Pig and human are excluded because of their special status.
The exclusion is established by other proofs, not denial of the hadith.

The Book of Purification. Skin of a dead animal. Even an elephant, because it is like other predators in the most correct opinion, because if it were [impure], one would not comb with a comb of ivory, which is the elephant's bone. And the skin of a dog is purified because it is not inherently impure in the correct opinion. (By actual tanning like qaraz) which is the leaves of the salam tree or the fruit of the sant tree, gallnuts, pomegranate peels, and alum. And (by) legal tanning like dusting, sunning, and exposure to air, so prayer is permissible in it and on it and ablution from it. He said: (And even an elephant: this is their view [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], and Muhammad said it is inherently impure like the pig because it is forbidden except if it is not benefited from. He said: because, etc.) i.e., this indicates the purity of its bone, and if it were like a pig, one would not comb with its bone. He said in al-Fath: This hadith invalidates Muhammad's statement of the elephant's inherent impurity. He said: (of ivory) in al-Muhkam: it is the elephant's tusks, and nothing other than the tusk is called ivory. Al-Jawhari said: it is the elephant's bone, one piece is called 'aja. This is what the author followed, and the term 'aj is also applied to the tail of the sea turtle, as al-Asma'i said and quoted by al-Sahib al-Misbah. The Shafi'is interpret the report that Fatima, may Allah be pleased with her, had a bracelet of ivory accordingly. He said: (because it is not inherently impure in the correct opinion). And the fatwa is upon it as in al-Bahr from al-Whabaniyya, because the apparent state of every animal is pure; it only becomes impure by death, and the impurity of its inside is in its stomach, so its ruling does not appear like the impurity of the inside of the prayer mat. Al-Nahr from al-Muhit. Some attribute it to the Imam, and the opinion of impurity to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad]. The effect of the difference appears in the case of one who prays with a small puppy in his sleeve: it is valid according to the first view, not the second. Al-Hindawani conditioned that it be closed-mouthed. He said: (by tanning) with kasra, it is what is used for tanning, and tanning is also the craft. He said: (like qaraz) with a pointed za; some mispronounce it with a dad; one piece is qaraza: a known seed that comes in a pod like lentils from the 'ada tree. He said: (which is the leaves of the salam tree) this is lax, for leaves are called khabt in their terminology; it is used as fodder, not for tanning. He said: (and alum) with a single ba, which is a mineral that Allah caused to grow in the earth, resembling vitriol. Al-Azhari said it. And 'shath' with triple tha is a plant with good fragrance and bitter taste used for tanning, as al-Jawhari said. Among actual tanning agents are salt and similar substances that remove odor and moisture, as in al-Qahistani. He added in al-Siraj: and prevent the return of corruption to the skin when water enters it. He said in al-Tabyin: if it dried and did not decompose (i.e., did not remove its stench, as al-Shalabi interpreted), it does not become pure. There is no difference in the tanner between Muslim, disbeliever, boy, insane person, and woman if the purpose of tanning is achieved. If a disbeliever tanned it and one strongly suspects he tanned it with something impure, then it must be washed. The moisture is forgiven as in al-Khulasa. In Minyat al-Musalli and its commentary: if a squirrel comes out of Dar al-Harb and it is known that it was tanned with the fat of a dead animal, prayer is not permissible with it until it is washed, because it was purified by tanning and became impure by the dead animal's fat. This is removed by washing and wringing if possible; otherwise, dry it three times. If it is known that it was tanned with something pure, prayer is permissible with it even if not washed. If in doubt, it is better to wash, and if not washed, it is permissible based on the principle of purity. In al-Qunyah: the hides tanned in our lands – their slaughterhouses are not washed, they do not avoid impurity in tanning, they throw them on impure ground, and they do not wash them after tanning – they are pure. It is permissible to make socks, slippers, book covers, combs, scabbards, and water skins from them, wet or dry. He said: (and sunning) in the gloss of al-Shalabi from al-Sakkaki, attributed to al-Hilya. Abu Nasr said: I heard some companions of Abu Hanifa say: it is purified by sunning only if the sun does the work of tanning. Then, tanning only works in a substance that accepts it; otherwise not, like the skin of a snake, mouse, and birds – they are not purified by it, just like meat. Similarly, they are not purified by slaughter because slaughter stands in place of tanning only for what can bear it. By birds that are not purified by slaughter are meant birds whose meat is not eaten; as for edible birds, their case is clear. The 'shirt' of a snake is pure as in al-Siraj and al-Bahr from al-Tajnis. He said: (so prayer is permissible in it) indicates that it is purified outwardly and inwardly. Malik said: only the outward is purified, so prayer is permissible on it but not in it, as in al-Tabyin. They differed on the permissibility of eating it after tanning if it is the skin of an edible animal; the correct view is that it is not permissible as in al-Siraj. He said: (Any hide that is tanned has become pure) – ihab is the skin before tanning; it is called that because it is prepared for tanning; its plural is ahab. After tanning it is called adim, plural adum. This hadith was recorded by al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, Ibn Hibban, al-Bazzar, and Ishaq from Ibn Abbas. He said: (Benefit from...) said in al-Fath: there is Ma'ruf ibn Hassan, unknown. He said: (except) the skin of the pig – Muhammad permitted the use of its hair due to necessity in his view; the two [Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf] forbade it because necessity is not established, as other materials substitute for it, as in al-Burhan. From Abu Yusuf in non-zahir al-riwayah: the skin of the pig is purified by tanning, and it is permissible to sell it, benefit from it, and pray in it and on it due to the generality of the hadith. The response is that the hadith means those not inherently impure, as in al-Halabi. He said: (and the skin of a human) even if a disbeliever, as in al-Qahistani, so it becomes pure but is not used. He said: (due to his honor, etc.) this suggests that the intended meaning of the negation of purity in the matn, understood from the exception, is its necessary consequence, which is the impermissibility of use, not the negation of purity in reality, because that would contradict honor, as al-Zayla'i stated.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi is a critical refinement of the ruling on purifying the skin of a dead animal (jild al-maytah) through tanning (dibagh). The commentator explains the positions of the Hanafi imams, the conditions for valid tanning, and the exceptions of pig and human skin.

The passage begins by stating that even the skin of an elephant is purified, because it is like other predators (siba') in the most correct opinion. This is followed by a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the elephant's skin is not inherently impure (najis al-'ayn), while Muhammad al-Shaybani considers it inherently impure like the pig. The proof for Abu Hanifa's view is that a comb made of ivory (elephant tusk) was used, which would not be permissible if the elephant were impure by nature. The correct view (al-sahih) is that it is purified, and the fatwa is upon it.

Similarly, the skin of a dog is purified because it is not inherently impure in the correct opinion. The passage then lists actual tanning agents (al-dibagha al-haqiqiyya) like qaraz, gallnuts, pomegranate peels, alum, and legal tanning (al-dibagha al-hukmiyya) like dusting, sunning, and air exposure. After proper tanning, prayer in or on the skin and ablution from it are permissible.

The commentator clarifies that tanning purifies both outwardly and inwardly, contrary to Imam Malik who held only outward purification. He also discusses the permissibility of eating the tanned skin of an edible animal: the correct view is that it is not permissible.

The proof is the hadith 'Any hide that is tanned has become pure' and the narration 'Benefit from the skins of dead animals when they are tanned.' These hadiths are cited to support the ruling.

The passage then addresses exceptions: the skin of the pig is not purified because it is inherently impure; Muhammad permitted its hair due to necessity, but the majority reject this. There is a weak report from Abu Yusuf that pig skin is purified, but it is not relied upon. Human skin is purified by tanning but cannot be used out of respect for human dignity.

Finally, the commentator notes that tanning requires a substance that accepts it; for example, the skin of snakes, mice, and non-edible birds does not become pure by tanning. The 'shirt' of a snake (its shed skin) is pure by itself.

Key Terms
إهاب (ihab): The skin of an animal before tanning; after tanning it is called adim.
دباغ (dibagh): A substance or process that removes moisture and odor from the skin, rendering it pure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the skin of a dead cow after it has been tanned?
A) It remains impure because the cow died naturally
B) It becomes pure and can be used for prayer
C) It becomes pure but only if tanned by a Muslim
D) It becomes pure only if the cow was slaughtered halally

Key Takeaway
Tanning is a means of purification for the skin of a dead animal, making it permissible for use in prayer and other acts requiring purity.

Taharah — Purification
advanced
12 min
Intra-Hanafi Disputes: Elephant, Dog, Pig, and Human Skin
Critical refinement of the tanning ruling: intra-Hanafi disputes on elephant, dog, pig, and human skin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why elephant and dog skins are purified in the relied-upon Hanafi view, why pig skin is not, and why human skin is purified but not used.

Core Idea
The elephant and dog are not inherently impure, so their skins are purified by tanning; the pig is impure by essence, and human skin is honored and thus not used.

The previous lesson established that tanning purifies dead animal skins. But what about elephant, dog, pig, or human? The Hanafi school has internal differences on some of these. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi disputes and the exceptions based on inherent impurity and human dignity.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule and the known exceptions
The general hadith covers all skins, but two exceptions are established: pig (najis al-ayn) and human (due to dignity). The disputed cases (elephant, dog) are included in the general rule by the majority.
The exceptions are not from the hadith but from other proofs.
2
Examine the elephant dispute
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the elephant's skin is purified because its bone (ivory) is used for combs, proving it is not inherently impure. Muhammad al-Shaybani considers it najis al-ayn like the pig because it is a predator not eaten.
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, supported by the practical evidence of ivory combs.
3
Analyze the dog dispute
Some early opinions considered dog skin not purified, but the correct view (sahih) in the later school is that dog skin is purified like other animals, as long as it is not a pig or human.
The effect of the dispute appears if one prays with a small dog in the sleeve; it is valid according to the correct view.
4
Understand the pig exception
Pig is excluded because it is najis al-ayn (intrinsically impure) by Quranic text. Using its hair is permitted only by Muhammad in case of necessity, but the majority forbid it.
A weak riwayah from Abu Yusuf that pig skin is purified is not relied upon.
5
Understand the human exception
Human skin becomes pure by tanning (since it becomes like any other dead animal skin), but it is not allowed to use because of human dignity (karamah).
This is based on respect for the human body, not on impurity.

The Book of Purification. Skin of a dead animal. Even an elephant, because it is like other predators in the most correct opinion, because if it were [impure], one would not comb with a comb of ivory, which is the elephant's bone. And the skin of a dog is purified because it is not inherently impure in the correct opinion. (By actual tanning like qaraz) which is the leaves of the salam tree or the fruit of the sant tree, gallnuts, pomegranate peels, and alum. And (by) legal tanning like dusting, sunning, and exposure to air, so prayer is permissible in it and on it and ablution from it. He said: (And even an elephant: this is their view [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], and Muhammad said it is inherently impure like the pig because it is forbidden except if it is not benefited from. He said: because, etc.) i.e., this indicates the purity of its bone, and if it were like a pig, one would not comb with its bone. He said in al-Fath: This hadith invalidates Muhammad's statement of the elephant's inherent impurity. He said: (of ivory) in al-Muhkam: it is the elephant's tusks, and nothing other than the tusk is called ivory. Al-Jawhari said: it is the elephant's bone, one piece is called 'aja. This is what the author followed, and the term 'aj is also applied to the tail of the sea turtle, as al-Asma'i said and quoted by al-Sahib al-Misbah. The Shafi'is interpret the report that Fatima, may Allah be pleased with her, had a bracelet of ivory accordingly. He said: (because it is not inherently impure in the correct opinion). And the fatwa is upon it as in al-Bahr from al-Whabaniyya, because the apparent state of every animal is pure; it only becomes impure by death, and the impurity of its inside is in its stomach, so its ruling does not appear like the impurity of the inside of the prayer mat. Al-Nahr from al-Muhit. Some attribute it to the Imam, and the opinion of impurity to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad]. The effect of the difference appears in the case of one who prays with a small puppy in his sleeve: it is valid according to the first view, not the second. Al-Hindawani conditioned that it be closed-mouthed. He said: (by tanning) with kasra, it is what is used for tanning, and tanning is also the craft. He said: (like qaraz) with a pointed za; some mispronounce it with a dad; one piece is qaraza: a known seed that comes in a pod like lentils from the 'ada tree. He said: (which is the leaves of the salam tree) this is lax, for leaves are called khabt in their terminology; it is used as fodder, not for tanning. He said: (and alum) with a single ba, which is a mineral that Allah caused to grow in the earth, resembling vitriol. Al-Azhari said it. And 'shath' with triple tha is a plant with good fragrance and bitter taste used for tanning, as al-Jawhari said. Among actual tanning agents are salt and similar substances that remove odor and moisture, as in al-Qahistani. He added in al-Siraj: and prevent the return of corruption to the skin when water enters it. He said in al-Tabyin: if it dried and did not decompose (i.e., did not remove its stench, as al-Shalabi interpreted), it does not become pure. There is no difference in the tanner between Muslim, disbeliever, boy, insane person, and woman if the purpose of tanning is achieved. If a disbeliever tanned it and one strongly suspects he tanned it with something impure, then it must be washed. The moisture is forgiven as in al-Khulasa. In Minyat al-Musalli and its commentary: if a squirrel comes out of Dar al-Harb and it is known that it was tanned with the fat of a dead animal, prayer is not permissible with it until it is washed, because it was purified by tanning and became impure by the dead animal's fat. This is removed by washing and wringing if possible; otherwise, dry it three times. If it is known that it was tanned with something pure, prayer is permissible with it even if not washed. If in doubt, it is better to wash, and if not washed, it is permissible based on the principle of purity. In al-Qunyah: the hides tanned in our lands – their slaughterhouses are not washed, they do not avoid impurity in tanning, they throw them on impure ground, and they do not wash them after tanning – they are pure. It is permissible to make socks, slippers, book covers, combs, scabbards, and water skins from them, wet or dry. He said: (and sunning) in the gloss of al-Shalabi from al-Sakkaki, attributed to al-Hilya. Abu Nasr said: I heard some companions of Abu Hanifa say: it is purified by sunning only if the sun does the work of tanning. Then, tanning only works in a substance that accepts it; otherwise not, like the skin of a snake, mouse, and birds – they are not purified by it, just like meat. Similarly, they are not purified by slaughter because slaughter stands in place of tanning only for what can bear it. By birds that are not purified by slaughter are meant birds whose meat is not eaten; as for edible birds, their case is clear. The 'shirt' of a snake is pure as in al-Siraj and al-Bahr from al-Tajnis. He said: (so prayer is permissible in it) indicates that it is purified outwardly and inwardly. Malik said: only the outward is purified, so prayer is permissible on it but not in it, as in al-Tabyin. They differed on the permissibility of eating it after tanning if it is the skin of an edible animal; the correct view is that it is not permissible as in al-Siraj. He said: (Any hide that is tanned has become pure) – ihab is the skin before tanning; it is called that because it is prepared for tanning; its plural is ahab. After tanning it is called adim, plural adum. This hadith was recorded by al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, Ibn Hibban, al-Bazzar, and Ishaq from Ibn Abbas. He said: (Benefit from...) said in al-Fath: there is Ma'ruf ibn Hassan, unknown. He said: (except) the skin of the pig – Muhammad permitted the use of its hair due to necessity in his view; the two [Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf] forbade it because necessity is not established, as other materials substitute for it, as in al-Burhan. From Abu Yusuf in non-zahir al-riwayah: the skin of the pig is purified by tanning, and it is permissible to sell it, benefit from it, and pray in it and on it due to the generality of the hadith. The response is that the hadith means those not inherently impure, as in al-Halabi. He said: (and the skin of a human) even if a disbeliever, as in al-Qahistani, so it becomes pure but is not used. He said: (due to his honor, etc.) this suggests that the intended meaning of the negation of purity in the matn, understood from the exception, is its necessary consequence, which is the impermissibility of use, not the negation of purity in reality, because that would contradict honor, as al-Zayla'i stated.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi is a critical refinement of the ruling on purifying the skin of a dead animal (jild al-maytah) through tanning (dibagh). The commentator explains the positions of the Hanafi imams, the conditions for valid tanning, and the exceptions of pig and human skin.

The passage begins by stating that even the skin of an elephant is purified, because it is like other predators (siba') in the most correct opinion. This is followed by a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the elephant's skin is not inherently impure (najis al-'ayn), while Muhammad al-Shaybani considers it inherently impure like the pig. The proof for Abu Hanifa's view is that a comb made of ivory (elephant tusk) was used, which would not be permissible if the elephant were impure by nature. The correct view (al-sahih) is that it is purified, and the fatwa is upon it.

Similarly, the skin of a dog is purified because it is not inherently impure in the correct opinion. The passage then lists actual tanning agents (al-dibagha al-haqiqiyya) like qaraz, gallnuts, pomegranate peels, alum, and legal tanning (al-dibagha al-hukmiyya) like dusting, sunning, and air exposure. After proper tanning, prayer in or on the skin and ablution from it are permissible.

The commentator clarifies that tanning purifies both outwardly and inwardly, contrary to Imam Malik who held only outward purification. He also discusses the permissibility of eating the tanned skin of an edible animal: the correct view is that it is not permissible.

The proof is the hadith 'Any hide that is tanned has become pure' and the narration 'Benefit from the skins of dead animals when they are tanned.' These hadiths are cited to support the ruling.

The passage then addresses exceptions: the skin of the pig is not purified because it is inherently impure; Muhammad permitted its hair due to necessity, but the majority reject this. There is a weak report from Abu Yusuf that pig skin is purified, but it is not relied upon. Human skin is purified by tanning but cannot be used out of respect for human dignity.

Finally, the commentator notes that tanning requires a substance that accepts it; for example, the skin of snakes, mice, and non-edible birds does not become pure by tanning. The 'shirt' of a snake (its shed skin) is pure by itself.

Key Terms
إهاب (ihab): The skin of an animal before tanning; after tanning it is called adim.
عاج (aj): Ivory, specifically the elephant's tusk; also used for turtle shell.
نجس العين (najis al-ayn): Intrinsically impure; a substance that is inherently impure regardless of external factors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, which animal's skin, if tanned, is considered pure but may not be used out of respect?
A) Pig
B) Dog
C) Elephant
D) Human

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school purifies most dead animal skins by tanning, with clear exceptions for pig (inherently impure) and human (honored), while elephant and dog are included in the general rule.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Tanning Purifies Hides Except Pig and Human Skin
Ruling on purification by tanning with specific exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purification by tanning, identify the exceptions for pig and human skin, and explain the rationale based on intrinsic impurity and human dignity.

Core Idea
Tanning purifies hides but not those intrinsically impure—pig skin remains impure; human skin becomes pure but cannot be used.

The purification of animal hides through tanning is a well-established ruling in the Hanafi school, based on a hadith. However, this rule does not apply universally: the pig's intrinsic impurity and the honor of the human body create exceptions. In this passage from a Hanafi commentary, we explore the proof, the exceptions, and clarify common misunderstandings, including intra-Hanafi differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary proof (dalil)
The hadith 'Any skin that is tanned has become purified' is cited as evidence. Note that it is a single hadith (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear.
The hadith is recorded by multiple scholars but is not mutawatir.
2
Understand the general ruling
Tanning removes impure moisture from the skin, making it pure. The process works with various materials like dirt, ash, salt.
The hide is considered originally pure; tanning extracts the impurity from the carcass.
3
Recognize the exceptions
Pig skin is excluded because it is intrinsically impure (najis al-ayn). Human skin is excluded due to honor, though it becomes pure.
Intrinsic impurity cannot be removed by tanning.
4
Note the differences within the school and with other madhahib
Some transmitted views permit use of pig hair or even tanning of pig skin, but the relied-upon view rejects these. Imam Malik holds tanning purifies only the outer surface.
The Hanafi masters (al-Halabi, al-Zayla'i) affirm the exceptions.
5
Apply the ruling to practical cases
Tanned leather from permissible animals is pure; if doubt arises, the default is purity, though washing is recommended.
The practice in some lands is to use tanned hides without washing.

أن يزيد صلاحه» (إلا جلد الخنزير) لنجاسة عينه، والدباغة لإخراج الرطوبة النجسة من الجلد الطاهر بالأصالة وهذا نجس العين (و) جلد (الآدمي) لحرمته صوناً له لكرامته وإن حكم بطهارته به لا يجوز استعماله كسائر أجزاء الآدمي يغسل لأنه طهر بالدباغ وتنجس بودك الميتة فيظهر بالغسل والعصر ان أمكن عصره، وإلا فيجفف ثلاثاً وإن علم أنه مدبوغ بشيء طاهر جازت معه الصلاة وإن لم يغسل وان شك فالأفضل أن يغسل ولو لم يغسل جازت بناء على أن الأصل الظهارة اهـ وفي القنية الجلود التي تدبغ في بلادنا، ولا يغسل مذبحها ولا تتوقى النجاسة في دبغها ويلقونها على الأرض النجسة، ولا يغسلونها بعد تمام الدبغ فهي طاهرة يجوز إتخاذ الخفاف، والمكاعب وغلاف الكتب والمشط والقراب والدلاء منها رطباً أو يابساً .اهـ. قوله : (والتشميس) في حاشية الشلبي عن السكاكي معزياً للحلية. قال أبو نصر : سمعت بعض أصحاب أبي حنيفة يقول : إنما يطهر بالتشميس إذا عملت الشمس به عمل الدباغ ،اهـ، ثم ان الدباغة لا تظهر إلا في محل يقبلها وإلا فلا كجلد الحية والفأرة، والطيور فإنها لا تطهر بها كاللحم وكذا لا تظهر بالذكاة لأنها إنما تقام مقام الدباغ فيما يحتمله والمراد بالطيور التي لا يظهر جلدها بالذكاة الطيور التي لا يؤكل لحمها أما المأكولة فأمرها ظاهر، وقميص الحية طاهر كما في السراج، والبحر عن التجنيس. قوله : (فتجوز الصلاة فيه أفاد به أنه طهر ظاهراً وباطناً، وقال مالك : يظهر الظاهر فقط فيصلى عليه لا فيه كما في التبيين واختلفوا في جواز أكله بعد الدبغ إذا كان جلد مأكول، والأصح أنه لا يجوز كما في السراج قوله : (أيما إهاب الخ الإهاب الجلد قبل الدبغ سمي به لأنه تهيأ للدبغ يقال : فلان تأهب للحرب إذا تهيأ وجمعه أهب بضمتين كحجاب وحجب، وهو بعد الدبغ أديم، وجمعه أدم بفتحتين كما في المغرب وغيره، ويسمى | أيضاً صرماً وجراباً وشناً كما في النهاية، والفتح وهذا الحديث أخرجه الترمذي والنسائي وابن ماجه والشافعي وأحمد وابن حبان والبزار واسحق من حديث ابن عباس قوله : (استمتعوا الخ) قال في الفتح : فيه معروف بن حسان مجهول قوله : (إلا) جلد الخنزير رخص محمد الإنتفاع بشعره لثبوت الضرورة عنده في ذلك ومنعاه لعدم تحققها لقيام غيره مقامه كما في البرهان، وعن أبي يوسف في غير ظاهر الرواية أن جلد الخنزير يطهر بالدباغ ويجوز بيعه والانتفاع به، والصلاة فيه وعليه لعموم الحديث، والجواب أن المراد غير نجس العين كما في الحلبي قوله: (وجلد الأدمي) ولو كافراً كما في القهستاني، فيطهر، ولا يستعل قوله : لكرامته الخ) فيه إشعار بأن المراد بنفي الطهارة في المصنف المعلوم من الاستثناء لازمها وهو عدم جواز الانتفاع لا نفي الطهارة حقيقة لأنه ينافي التكريم كما أفاده الزيلعي .
For his statement: 'Any skin that is tanned has become purified.' And he intended to perform ablution from a waterskin, and it was said to him: 'It is from a carcass,' so he said: 'Its tanning removes filth, or impurity, or uncleanliness.' And he said: 'Benefit from the skins of carcasses when they are tanned, whether with dirt, ash, salt, or anything else, as long as it increases their goodness.' Except the skin of the pig, due to its intrinsic impurity. Tanning is for extracting the impure moisture from the skin, which is originally pure, but this is impure in itself. And the skin of a human being, due to its sanctity, as a preservative for his honor. Even if it is ruled pure by tanning, it is not permissible to use it, like other parts of the human being. It is washed because it became pure by tanning but was contaminated by the fat of the carcass, so it becomes apparent by washing and wringing if possible; otherwise, it is dried three times. And if he knows it was tanned with something pure, prayer is valid with it even if not washed; if in doubt, it is better to wash; even if not washed, it is valid based on the original presumption of purity. In al-Quniya: the skins tanned in our lands, their slaughter place is not washed, nor does one avoid impurity in their tanning, and they are thrown on impure ground, and they are not washed after tanning; they are pure, and it is permissible to make from them slippers, combs, book covers, combs, sheaths, and buckets, wet or dry. His statement: 'And sun-drying'—in the marginalia of al-Shalabi from al-Sakkaki attributing to al-Hilya: Abu Nasr said: I heard some companions of Abu Hanifa say: 'It is only purified by sun-drying if the sun does the work of tanning.' Then tanning only takes effect in what accepts it, otherwise not, like the skin of a snake, mouse, and birds; they are not purified by it like meat, nor are they purified by slaughter, because it serves the place of tanning in what it can bear. What is meant by birds whose skin is not purified by slaughter are those whose meat is not eaten; as for edible birds, their matter is clear. And the shirt of a snake is pure as in al-Siraj and al-Bahr from al-Tajnis. His statement: 'So prayer is permissible in it' indicates it is pure outwardly and inwardly; Malik said: 'It purifies only the outside, so prayer on it is valid, not in it.' And they differed about the permissibility of eating it after tanning if the skin is from an edible animal; the correct view is that it is not permissible as in al-Siraj. His statement: 'Any skin (ihāb)'—ihāb is the skin before tanning, so named because it is prepared for tanning; it is said: 'So-and-so prepared for war' when he readied himself; its plural is 'ahb' with two dammas like hijaab/hujub. After tanning it is 'adīm', its plural is 'adam' with two fatahas as in al-Mughrib and others. It is also called 'sarm', 'jirāb', and 'shann' as in al-Nihaya. This hadith is transmitted by al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, Ibn Hibban, al-Bazzar, and Ishaq from Ibn Abbas. His statement: 'Benefit (istamti'ū)'—al-Fath said: In it is Ma'ruf ibn Hassan, unknown. His statement: 'Except the skin of the pig'—Muhammad permitted using its hair due to established necessity in his view, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) forbid it because necessity is not established since something else suffices. And from Abu Yusuf, in non-Zahir al-Riwaya, that pig skin is purified by tanning, and its sale and use is permitted, and prayer is valid in and on it due to the generality of the hadith. The response is that what is meant is other than intrinsically impure things. His statement: 'And the skin of the human being'—even if an unbeliever as in al-Qahistani; it becomes pure, but it is not used. His statement: 'due to his honor etc.' suggests that what is meant by negating purity in the text, known from the exception, is its necessary consequence, which is impermissibility of use, not negation of purity in reality, because that would contradict honor as stated by al-Zayla'i.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This is a passage from a Hanafi commentary (hashiya) on the ruling of purification by tanning. The text opens with a hadith: 'Any skin that is tanned has become purified.' This is the primary proof. The passage then explains that tanning removes the impure moisture from a skin that was originally pure. An exception is made for pig skin because it is intrinsically impure (najis al-ayn); tanning cannot remove essential impurity. Human skin is also excepted—though tanning renders it pure, it may not be used due to the honor of the human being. The commentary then details that after tanning, the hide should be washed to remove any residual fat from the carcass. The common practice in their lands (presumably Egypt or India) is described: tanned hides are considered pure even without washing, and many items can be made from them. The passage also discusses sun-drying (tashmis) as an alternative to tanning, and notes that tanning only works on hides that can accept it—like the skin of snakes, mice, and birds (which are not edible) cannot be purified by tanning or slaughter. An inter-madhhab difference with Imam Malik is noted: Malik holds that tanning purifies only the outer surface, while the Hanafi view is that it purifies both outwardly and inwardly. The commentary also addresses a lesser-known transmission (nawadir) of Imam Muhammad permitting use of pig hair due to necessity, and a non-Zahir al-Riwaya view of Abu Yusuf that pig skin is purified by tanning. The Hashiya corrects these by stating that the hadith applies only to hides that are not intrinsically impure. Finally, human skin is discussed: it becomes pure by tanning, but its use is prohibited because of human dignity; negating purity would contradict that honor.

Key Terms
إهاب (ihāb): Skin before tanning; prepared for the tanning process.
دباغ (dabāgh): The process of treating a hide with substances like ash, salt, or dirt to extract impure moisture and render it pure.
نجس العين (najis al-ʿayn): An object that is inherently impure regardless of external factors, such as the pig.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is pig skin not purified by tanning according to the Hanafi school?
A) The pig is a filthy animal and its skin cannot be tanned.
B) Pig skin is intrinsically impure (najis al-ayn), so tanning cannot remove its essential impurity.
C) The hadith only applies to skins of animals that are edible.
D) Tanning only works on skins that have been slaughtered properly, and pigs are not slaughtered.

Key Takeaway
Tanning purifies hides of permissible animals, but pig skin remains impure due to intrinsic impurity, and human skin, though purified, cannot be used due to honor.
